Does Permission for Marriage Include Defective Marriage?
Dispute on master's permission scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a master's absolute permission for marriage covers defective contracts, identify the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and state the mufta bihi for direct marriage versus agency.

Core Idea
Absolute permission extends to both valid and defective marriage per Abu Hanifa, but the fatwa for agency restricts to valid.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the scope of a master's permission for a slave to marry is debated between Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples. Does an unconditional command like 'Marry this woman' include a defective marriage (e.g., without witnesses)? The answer affects when the mahr becomes due and whether the permission is exhausted. This lesson unpacks the arguments and practical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The issue is whether a master's absolute permission for marriage (e.g., 'Marry this woman') extends to a defective (fasid) marriage contract. Abu Hanifa says yes; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani say only valid marriage is covered. Note that this is an intra-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school.
The Sahibayn restrict permission because the purpose (chastity) is achieved only by valid marriage.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's evidence
Abu Hanifa argues that the contract is unrestricted by any description of validity or invalidity, so it runs on its absolute meaning. He analogizes to sale, which includes both valid and fasid. Moreover, a fasid marriage still produces some legal effects: lineage, mahr, 'iddah, and avoiding hadd. Therefore, the permission covers it.
The key principle: al-'umur bi-maqasidiha? Here it's about absolute wording.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's evidence
The Sahibayn focus on the objective of marriage: chastity and protection (ihsan). A fasid marriage does not provide permissibility (hillah); it is haram to continue. They compare it to agency for marriage (tawkil), which only covers valid marriage. Also, in oaths about the future, custom restricts to valid marriage.
They also point out that in sale, fasid conveys ownership, but marriage does not yield halal intimacy, so the analogy breaks.
4
Analyze the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference appears in two areas: (1) liability for mahr: Abu Hanifa says the slave is sold immediately to pay the mahr; the Sahibayn say the mahr is only due after manumission. (2) Termination of permission: if the slave marries another (or repeats), according to Abu Hanifa the permission is exhausted; according to the Sahibayn it is not, so the second marriage is valid.
This shows that the dispute is not merely theoretical but affects financial and marital status.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for each context
For a slave marrying himself directly, Abu Hanifa's view is considered strong. However, for agency (tazwij), where someone is appointed to give the slave in marriage, the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view: agency only includes valid marriage. The text explicitly says 'and upon this is the fatwa' for tazwij.
Thus, the operative ruling varies by scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإذن بالنكاح يتناول الفاسد: حتى لو قال لعبده تزوج هذه المرأة، فتزوجها نكاحاً فاسداً ودخل بها، فإنه يباع فيه في الحال أبي حنيفة عملاً بإطلاق اللفظ وقالا : لا يتناول إلا الصحيح؛ لأن مقصوده بالنكاح الإعفاف والتحصين، وذلك بالجائز لا الفاسد؛ لأنه لا يفيد الحل، فصار كالتوكيل بالنكاح، فإنه يتناول الجائز دون الفاسد، ففي الصورة المذكورة لا يلزمه المهر، ولا يباع فيه في الحال، بل يؤخذ منه إذا أعتق، ولهذا لو حلف: "لا يتزوج" ينصرف إلى الجائز، بخلاف البيع حيث يتناول الجائز والفاسد؛ لأن الفاسد فيه يفيد الملك بالقبض، ولأبي حنيفة : - إن العقد غير مقيد بوصف الصحة أو الفساد فيجري على إطلاقه، فكان كالبيع وبعض المقاصد حاصل بالنكاح الفاسد كثبوت النسب وسقوط الحد ووجوب المهر والعدة ومسألة اليمين إن أسلم فالأيمان مبنية على العرف، والعرف بالصحيح دون الفاسد في المستقبل، نعم لو حلف أنه ما تزوج في الماضي يتناول الصحيح والفاسد، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في لزوم المهر عنده في الحال وعندهما لا يطالب إلا بعد العتق كما بينا، وفي حق انتهاء الإذن بالعقد حتى لو تزوج غيرها نكاحاً صحيحاً أو أعاد عليه العقد صح عندهما؛ لأنه لم ينته الإذن، وعنده لا يصح؛ لانتهاء الإذن، وهذا الخلاف في التزوج، وأما في التزويج أي إن وكل شخصاً : بتزويجه لا يتناول الفاسد، وعليه الفتوى.
And permission for marriage includes the defective: such that if he said to his slave, 'Marry this woman,' and he married her with a defective marriage and had intercourse with her, then according to Abu Hanifa he is sold immediately for the mahr, acting upon the absolute wording [of the permission]. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: Permission only includes valid marriage; because his intent in marriage is chastity and protection, and that is achieved by valid marriage, not defective, because it does not provide permissibility. So it is like agency for marriage, which includes valid but not defective. Thus in the mentioned case, the mahr is not incumbent on him, nor is he sold immediately, but it is taken from him when he is freed. For this reason, if he swears, 'I will not marry,' it applies to valid marriage only. Unlike sale, which includes both valid and defective, because defective sale conveys ownership by possession. Abu Hanifa's proof: The contract is not restricted by a description of validity or invalidity, so it runs on its absolute meaning, like sale. And some objectives are achieved by defective marriage, such as establishing lineage, avoiding the hadd penalty, obligation of mahr and 'iddah. Regarding oaths: if he embraces Islam, then oaths are based on custom, and custom is with valid marriage, not defective in the future. However, if he swears that he did not marry in the past, it includes both valid and defective. The practical result of the difference appears in the immediate liability for mahr according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them it is only demanded after manumission. And regarding the termination of permission: if he marries another validly or repeats the contract, it is valid according to them because the permission has not ended; according to Abu Hanifa it is not valid because the permission has ended. This difference is in the slave marrying himself. As for giving in marriage (tazwij), if he appoints someone to give him in marriage, it does not include defective, and upon this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether a master's permission for his slave to marry includes only a valid marriage or also a defective one. Abu Hanifa argues that the absolute wording covers both, and some benefits (e.g., nasab, mahr, 'iddah) occur even in defective marriage. The Sahibayn restrict the permission to valid marriage because the purpose is chastity, which only valid marriage provides. The practical difference is when the mahr becomes due: Abu Hanifa says immediately, the Sahibayn say only after manumission. Also, the permission is exhausted after one marriage according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn consider it continuous. In matters of agency for marriage (tazwij), the ruling follows the Sahibayn's view that it does not cover defective marriage, and this is the fatwa position.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage contract that is defective due to a missing condition (e.g., without witnesses), but produces some legal effects (lineage, mahr, 'iddah) if consummated.
تزويج (tazwīj): Giving in marriage, i.e., acting as a guardian or agent to marry someone off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it includes both valid and defective.
B) No, it only includes valid marriage.
C) It depends on the wording.
D) Only if the agent is explicitly told.

Key Takeaway
The scope of permission for marriage varies by context: direct permission includes both valid and defective per Abu Hanifa, but agency (tazwij) follows the Sahibayn's restriction to valid only.

No Obligation of Tabwiyah for Married Female Slave
Ruling on master's right to service of married slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tabwiyah and distinguish it from the positions of al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Core Idea
A master's right to his slave's service outranks the husband's right to exclusive cohabitation.

This passage explains that when a master marries his female slave, he is not required to provide the husband with exclusive access (tabwiyah). The master may continue to employ her, and even a contractual stipulation of tabwiyah is not binding. However, if the master voluntarily grants exclusive residence, the husband must provide maintenance. The lesson also compares the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states clearly: 'Tabwiyah is not obligatory.' Recognize that this is the default ruling for the master.
Tabwiyah means vacating a dwelling for the couple and ceasing to employ her.
2
Understand the reasoning
The master's right is stronger because he owns her person and usufructs; tabwiyah would nullify his right to service. This is the basis for the non-obligation.
The husband's right is limited to intercourse if he finds her available.
3
Note the exceptions and secondary rulings
If the master voluntarily provides tabwiyah, the husband must pay maintenance. The master may later resume employing her. The mukatabah (slave with a contract of emancipation) is treated like a free woman and needs no tabwiyah.
Even a stipulation of tabwiyah in the marriage contract is not binding.
4
Compare with other schools
Al-Shafi'i requires the master to surrender her at night; Malik after three nights. These are not followed in the Hanafi school.
The Hanafi position is based on the priority of the master's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجب تبويتها : [ أي اتخاذ المنزل لها . بوأ له منزلاً و بوأه منزلاً إذا هيأله. (ع)] المراد بالتبوية ههنا أن يخلي بينها وبينه ويسلمها إليه ولا يستخدمها، فإن كانت تذهب وتجيء وتخدم المولى لا يكون تبوية، وقوله : "فتخدمه ويطأ الزوج إن ظفر بها" علة لعدم وجوب التبوية؛ لأن حق المولى أقوى من حق الزوج، والتبوية تبطل حق الاستخدام، فلا يجب؛ لأن المولى يملك ذاتها ومنافعها، ولهذا يدخل فيه ملك المتعة تبعاً، ولا كذلك الزوج، وكذا إن شرط التبوية لا تجب؛ لأن هذه الشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، فإن بوأها معه منزلاً فلها النفقة والسكنى على الزوج؛ لأن النفقة تقابل الاحتباس، وإلا فلا نفقة على الزوج، ولو بدا له أن يستخدمها بعد التبوية فله ذلك؛ لأن حقه لا يسقط بالتبوية كما لا يسقط بالنكاح. ولو طلقها بائنا بعد التبوية يجب لها النفقة والسكنى، وقبلها أو بعد الاسترداد لا يجب والمكاتبة إذا تزوجت بإذن المولى فإنها كالحرة ولا تحتاج إلى التبوية؛ لزوال يد المولى عنها، وعند الشافعي: يسلم المولى الأمة المنكوحة إلى الزوج ليلاً ويستخدمها نهاراً، وعند مالك ليلاً بعد ثلاث ليال. (عيني، فتح)
Tabwiyah is not obligatory. The intended meaning of tabwiyah here is that he vacates [the house] for her and surrenders her to him and does not employ her. If she goes and comes and serves the master, that is not tabwiyah. His statement: 'She serves him and the husband has intercourse with her if he finds her' is the reason for the non-obligation of tabwiyah, because the master's right is stronger than the husband's right, and tabwiyah nullifies the right of service, so it is not obligatory; because the master owns her person and her usufructs, and for this reason ownership of enjoyment enters it as a subsidiary, but not so the husband. Likewise, if he stipulates tabwiyah, it is not obligatory, because this stipulation is not required by the contract. If he does vacate a house with him, then she has maintenance and lodging on the husband, because maintenance is in exchange for confinement; otherwise there is no maintenance on the husband. If he later decides to employ her after tabwiyah, he may do so, because his right is not nullified by tabwiyah just as it is not nullified by marriage. If he divorces her irrevocably after tabwiyah, maintenance and lodging are due to her; before tabwiyah or after reclaiming [her service] it is not due. The mukatabah, if she marries with the master's permission, is like a free woman and does not need tabwiyah because the master's hand is removed from her. According to al-Shafi'i: the master surrenders the married slave woman to the husband at night and employs her during the day. According to Malik: at night after three nights.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained by the commentator. It addresses the situation where a master marries his female slave to a man. The key issue is whether the master must provide the husband exclusive access to the wife (tabwiyah) by ceasing to employ her. The ruling is clear: tabwiyah is not obligatory. The master retains the right to her service because he owns her person and usufructs, a right that takes precedence over the husband's marital rights. Even if the master stipulates tabwiyah in the contract, it is not binding. However, if he voluntarily gives her exclusive residence, then the husband must provide maintenance. The master can later resume employing her at will. The mukatabah (slave woman contracted for emancipation) is an exception; she is like a free woman after permission, so no tabwiyah is needed. The passage also notes the positions of al-Shafi'i and Malik as contrasts.

Key Terms
تبوية (tabwiyah): The act of vacating a dwelling for the married couple, i.e., giving the wife exclusive residence with her husband and ceasing to employ her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a master marries his female slave and then stipulates in the contract that she will have exclusive residence (tabwiyah)?
A) The stipulation is binding and must be fulfilled.
B) The stipulation is not binding and can be ignored.
C) The stipulation is void and the marriage is invalid.
D) The stipulation only applies if the husband pays extra mahr.

Key Takeaway
The master's ownership right over a female slave's service takes precedence over the husband's right to exclusive cohabitation; tabwiyah is not obligatory in the Hanafi school.

Dispute on Idhn Scope for Ma'dhun Slave Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether fasid marriage consumes slave's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether a fasid marriage consumes the idhn of a ma'dhun slave, and state the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
A fasid marriage consumes idhn according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn — this determines when mahr is due and whether later marriages are valid.

When a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade) marries, his wife becomes equal with his creditors in claiming her mahr. But what if the marriage is fasid (invalid)? Does that still use up the master's permission to marry? This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on this question, and traces the practical consequences for mahr and subsequent marriages.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the position of Abu Hanifa and the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether a fasid marriage consumes the slave's permission (idhn).
Abu Hanifa: yes, any marriage, valid or fasid, consumes permission. Sahibayn: only a valid marriage consumes permission.
2
Identify the dalil of each
Find the reasoning each side gives. Abu Hanifa argues the contract is absolute and not restricted by validity; the Sahibayn argue that only a valid marriage achieves full objectives.
The text says: 'the contract is not restricted by a description of validity or invalidity.'
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequences: when is mahr due? Is a second valid marriage permissible without new permission?
Immediate mahr vs deferred after emancipation; validity of second marriage.
4
Distinguish tazawwuj from tazwīj
Note that the dispute is about the slave marrying himself (tazawwuj). For giving in marriage (tazwīj), the fatwa is that fasid is not included.
The text ends with 'وعليه الفتوى' for tazwīj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زوّج عبداً مأذوناً امرأة صح وهي أسوة للغرماء في مهرها، ومن زوّج أي المولى مديونا بمهر المثل أو أقل أي النكاح أي المرأة مساوية أمته لا يجب تبويتها فتخدمه ويطأ الزوج أي أم ولد من رجل أي تخدم الجارية المولى = إن العقد غير مقيد بوصف الصحة أو الفساد فيجري على إطلاقه، فكان كالبيع وبعض المقاصد حاصل بالنكاح الفاسد كثبوت النسب وسقوط الحد ووجوب المهر والعدة ومسألة اليمين إن أسلم فالأيمان مبنية على العرف، والعرف بالصحيح دون الفاسد في المستقبل، نعم لو حلف أنه ما تزوج في الماضي يتناول الصحيح والفاسد، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في لزوم المهر عنده في الحال وعندهما لا يطالب إلا بعد العتق كما بينا، وفي حق انتهاء الإذن بالعقد حتى لو تزوج غيرها نكاحاً صحيحاً أو أعاد عليه العقد صح عندهما؛ لأنه لم ينته الإذن، وعنده لا يصح؛ لانتهاء الإذن، وهذا الخلاف في التزوج، وأما في التزويج أي إن وكل شخصاً : بتزويجه لا يتناول الفاسد، وعليه الفتوى. (عيني، فتح) بلا مهر من أيضاً كما يتناول الصحيح فيباع فيه إذا وطيها. (ع) صح وهي أسوة: أما صحة النكاح فلأنه يبتني على ملك الرقبة، فيجوز تحصيناً للعبد، وأما المهر فلأنه لزمه حكماً بسبب لا مردّ له، وهو صحة النكاح؛ إذ هو غير مشروع، فصار كدين الاستهلاك وكالمريض إذا تزوج امرأة فبقدر مهر مثلها تكون أسوة للغرماء، وهذا إذا كان النكاح بمهر المثل أو أقل منه؛ لأنه لو زاد على قدر مهر المثل لم تكن المرأة أسوة للغرماء في ما زاد، بل يؤخر حقها إلى استيفاء الغرماء ديونهم كدين الصحة مع دين المرض، والحاصل أنه ليس للغرماء منع المولى عن تزويج عبده المأذون المديون؛ لأن المولى مالك الرقبة، والنكاح لا يلاقي حق الغرماء بالإبطال قصداً، لكن بعد صحة النكاح وجب الدين بسبب لا يقبل الرد فشابه دين استهلاك العبد المأذون شيئاً يجب قيمته. (مستخلص، فتح) للغرماء: جمع غريم بمعنى الدائن أي أصحاب الديون التي على العبد. (عيني، فتح) مهرها: فيباع في الكل ويقسم بينهم وبينهما على قدر الحقوق. (ط)
If he marries a ma'dhun slave to a woman, it is valid and she is equal with the creditors in her mahr. The master, if he marries his indebted slave woman with mahr al-mithl or less, [then] his female slave does not require tabwiyah; she serves him and the husband has intercourse if he finds her. The contract is not restricted by a description of validity or invalidity, so it proceeds upon its absolute nature. Thus it is like sale, and some objectives are achieved by a fasid marriage, such as establishment of lineage, dropping of hadd, obligation of mahr and iddah. And the issue of the oath: if he embraces Islam, oaths are based on custom, and custom is with the valid, not the invalid, in the future. However, if he swears that he has not married in the past, it includes the valid and the invalid. The fruit of the dispute appears in the obligation of the mahr immediately according to him (Abu Hanifa), and according to them (Sahibayn) it is not demanded until after emancipation as we explained. And regarding the termination of permission by the contract: if he marries another woman validly or repeats the contract, it is valid according to them, because permission has not expired; according to him it is invalid, because permission has expired. This dispute is in marriage (tazawwuj). As for giving in marriage (tazwīj): if he appoints someone to give him in marriage, it does not include the fasid, and upon that is the fatwa. Without mahr, it also includes the valid, so he is sold if he has intercourse with her. The validity of the marriage is because it is based on ownership of the neck, so it is permitted for the protection of the slave. As for the mahr, it is because it is incumbent upon him due to an irrevocable cause, that is the validity of the marriage, since it is not [possession] without right, so it becomes like a consumption debt. And like a sick person if he marries a woman, then to the extent of mahr al-mithl she is equal with creditors. This is if the marriage is with mahr al-mithl or less; if he exceeds the mahr al-mithl, the woman is not equal with creditors in the excess, rather her right is deferred until the creditors' debts are satisfied, like the debt of health with the debt of illness. The summary is: the creditors cannot prevent the master from marrying his ma'dhun indebted slave, because the master owns the neck, and marriage does not confront the creditors' right with intended nullification. But after the marriage is valid, a debt is due by an irrevocable cause, so it resembles a consumption debt of the ma'dhun slave. The creditors are the owners of the debts upon the slave. Her mahr: the slave is sold wholly and divided among them and her according to the proportion of rights.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of a master's permission (idhn) for his ma'dhun slave to marry. The background: a ma'dhun slave is permitted to trade; if he also marries, the wife is equal with his creditors in her mahr claim if the mahr is not excessive. The core dispute is whether a fasid (invalid) marriage consumes the permission such that the slave cannot marry again without new permission. Abu Hanifa (ra) holds that any marriage, valid or invalid, uses up the permission because the contract is considered absolutely. Therefore, if the slave marries invalidly, he cannot marry again validly until new permission is given. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only a valid marriage consumes permission; an invalid marriage does not, so subsequent valid marriages are valid. The practical consequence (thamarah) is twofold: (1) mahr becomes due immediately according to Abu Hanifa even before emancipation, while the Sahibayn say it is only demanded after emancipation; (2) the permission is terminated or not. The text also notes that in the case of giving in marriage (tazwīj) where a man appoints someone to give him in marriage, the authorization does not cover a fasid marriage; that is the relied-upon position. The passage also explains why the wife is equal with creditors: because the mahr is a debt incurred by a valid marriage, which is irrevocable, like a consumption debt. The creditors cannot prevent the marriage itself because the master owns the slave.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Master's permission for the slave to engage in a legal act, here marriage.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage that lacks a condition but still produces some legal effects (mahr, iddah, lineage).
أسوة الغرماء (uswah al-ghurama'): Being equal with the creditors in claims against the debtor's assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a ma'dhun slave marries in a fasid marriage and then marries another woman in a valid marriage without new permission, what is the ruling?
A) Both marriages are valid.
B) The second marriage is invalid because permission was consumed by the first.
C) The first marriage is invalid, so permission remains for the second.
D) Both marriages are invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a fasid marriage consumes the slave's permission to marry according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn; the fatwa for tazwīj excludes fasid.

Master's Right to Compel Slave to Marry
Ruling with proof on master's ijbar over slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's ijbar over slaves and explain the proof and refutation of the opposing view.

Core Idea
The master's full ownership grants authority to compel marriage for both male and female slaves.

This passage expands on the master's right to force his slaves into marriage, a ruling based on complete ownership of the person (raqabah) and its usufructs. It addresses a cross-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i and clarifies that the master cannot compel a mukatab slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text begins by stating that the master has the right to compel both male and female slaves (al-‘abd wa al-amah) to marry, without their consent.
This is the straightforward Hanafi position.
2
Understand the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i (and a weak Hanafi narration) holds that the male slave cannot be compelled because he retains his humanity in matters like marriage, which are personal.
The opposing evidence includes that the master cannot divorce the slave's wife or make qisas claims.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof states: the slave is owned in person (raqabah) and hand (yad). Thus the master has full authority over all dispositions that safeguard his ownership, just like selling or marrying.
This is qiyas jaliy: the male slave is like the female slave in being owned.
4
Evaluate the rebuttal
The passage refutes the opposing analogy by showing that if partial humanity prevented compulsion, then the slave would own what the master does not own, but in fact the master owns financial matters and the slave does not.
Thus the analogy is false; the master's ownership is comprehensive.
5
Distinguish the exception
The mukatab (slave under contract of kitabah) cannot be compelled because they are like free persons in many legal aspects, as they are entitled to compensation and the mukatabah earns mahr.
This exception holds even if the mukatab is a minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله إجبارهما : [ أي العبد والأمة، ومعنى الإجبار أن ينفذ النكاح عليهما بغير رضائهما. (ع)] وقال الشافعي: لا إجبار في العبد، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف؛ لأنه مبقى على أصل الآدمية فيما هو من خصائص الآدمية، والنكاح منها، ولا يدخل في ملكه إلا ماليته ولا تعلق لها بالنكاح، فكان أجنبيا عن المولى في إنكاحه، ألا ترى أنه لا يملك الإقرار عليه بالقصاص ولا أن يطلق عليه امرأته؛ لكونه أجنبيا، بخلاف الأمة؛ لأن بضعها مملوك له فيملك تمليكه، ولنا: أن العبد مملوكه رقبةً ويداً فيملك عليه كل تصرف فيه صيانة ملكه كالأمة. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢١/١] وهو مالك تزويج الأمة؛ لكونها مملوكة له رقبة ويدا لا لأنه لا يملك بضعها ولا تأثير لملك البضع فيه ولا لعدمه، ألا ترى أنه ليس له أن يزوج امرأته وإن كان يملك بضعها، وله أن يزوج ابنته وإن كان لا يملك بضعها، فلا تأثير لما ذكره طردا وعكسا وما ذكر من أنه مبقى على أصل الآدمية لعدم ملكه فاسد؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لملكه العبد؛ لأن ما لا يملكه المولى يملكه العبد كالإقرار بالحدود والقصاص، وما لا يملكه العبد يملكه المولى كالإقرار عليه بالمال، فعلم أن قياسه باطل ولا يملك المولى إجبار المكاتب والمكاتبة؛ لأنهما التحقا بالأجانب بعقد الكتابة ولهذا يستحقان الأرش على المولى بالجناية عليهما وتستحق المكاتبة المهر إذا وطيها المولى فصار كالحرين، فلا يجبران على النكاح وإن كانا صغيرين (فتح) وله إجبارهما : للمولى الكامل الملك ولو صغيراً.
He has the right to compel both [the male slave and the slave woman]. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no compulsion regarding the male slave, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; because he is kept upon the original state of humanity in what is from the characteristics of humanity, and marriage is from it, and only his financial value enters into ownership, which has no connection to marriage. Thus the master is a stranger regarding giving him in marriage. Do you not see that he cannot make an acknowledgment against him for qisas, nor divorce his wife, because he is a stranger, unlike the slave woman because her sexual organ is owned by him so he can own its transfer. Our proof: the male slave is owned by him in person and hand, so he has authority over him in every disposition that safeguards his ownership, just like the slave woman. He is the owner of giving the slave woman in marriage because she is owned by him in person and hand, not because he does not own her sexual organ, and the ownership of the sexual organ has no effect on this, positively or negatively. Do you not see that he cannot give his wife in marriage even though he owns her sexual organ, and he can give his daughter in marriage even though he does not own her sexual organ? Thus what he mentioned has no effect, positively or conversely. What he mentioned about being kept on the original state of humanity due to lack of ownership is false; because if it were so, the slave would own it, since what the master does not own, the slave owns, like acknowledgment of hadd and qisas, and what the slave does not own, the master owns, like acknowledgment of financial liability against him. Hence it is known that his analogy is invalid. The master does not have the right to compel the mukatab and mukatabah, because they have become like strangers by the contract of kitabah; therefore they are entitled to compensation from the master for injury upon them, and the mukatabah is entitled to mahr if the master has intercourse with her; thus they become like free persons, so they are not compelled to marry, even if they are minors. He has the right to compel: for the master with complete ownership, even if the master is a minor.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding the master's right to compel his slaves to marry. The Hanafi position is that the master can enforce the marriage of both his male and female slaves without their consent, because full ownership of the person (raqabah) and its usufructs gives the master authority over all dispositions that safeguard his property. This includes marriage since it benefits the slave and preserves the master's interest (e.g., preventing zina). The passage rebuts Shafi'i's view and a weak narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that only the female slave can be compelled because her sexual organ is owned, while the male slave is like a free person regarding marriage. The Hanafi response: the male slave is fully owned, so the master's authority extends to him just as to the female. The analogy of the free man is rejected because the master does not own a free man. The passage also distinguishes mukatab slaves (those under contract of emancipation) who cannot be compelled because they are like free persons. The ruling applies even if the master is a minor, as long as he has complete ownership.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbar): The master's authority to enforce a marriage contract upon his slave without the slave's consent.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of emancipation (kitabah) and pays installments; he is on the path to freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a master compel his male slave to marry?
A) Yes, because the master owns the slave's person entirely.
B) Yes, but only if the slave is indebted.
C) No, because the male slave's humanity is paramount.
D) No, only female slaves can be compelled.

Key Takeaway
The master's complete ownership (raqabah and yad) gives him the right to compel both male and female slaves to marry, except for mukatab slaves.

Drop of Mahr When Master Kills His Slave Woman: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr drop when master kills slave wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mahr drop under Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, identify the conditions that trigger the disagreement, and apply the correct ruling in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Mahr drops only if the master (who receives it) destroys his unpermitted slave wife before consummation—otherwise it remains due.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi marriage law: a master kills his own slave woman who is married but not authorized by him, before consummation. The central question is whether the husband still owes the mahr. The two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagree with Abu Hanifa. The dispute hinges on whether the master's act is treated as a forfeiture of his right to the mahr or merely as a natural death that does not affect the husband's obligation. The text also clarifies conditions and exceptions, making it a rich case study for understanding the role of intent and causation in contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
The passage states that the mahr drops if the master kills his own slave woman before consummation, provided she is not authorized to marry and the master is a sane adult. Identify these conditions from the text.
Note the qualifiers: 'before intercourse', 'his slave woman', 'not authorized'.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text explicitly contrasts Abu Hanifa's position (mahr drops) with that of the Sahibayn (mahr does not drop). Identify their respective proofs as presented.
Abu Hanifa's proof: the master forfeited the object of the contract. Sahibayn's proof: death is death; the mahr was established at contract.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
Examine how Abu Hanifa uses analogy: killing is treated as destruction in worldly rulings (qisas, diyah, disinheritance), so similarly the mahr is forfeit. The Sahibayn analogize to a natural death. Understand the logical structure.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the ruling is derived from a broader principle.
4
Note the exceptions and consensus areas
The text mentions several cases where mahr does not drop: if someone else kills her, if she kills herself, if she was authorized, if she is a mukatabah, after consummation, or if the killer is a minor/insane.
These exceptions help define the precise scope of Abu Hanifa's ruling.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute leads to a concrete difference: under Abu Hanifa the husband pays nothing; under the Sahibayn he must still pay the mahr to the master's estate. Consider which view is more equitable.
The text does not give a tarjih (preference), leaving the mufti to weigh the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته قبل الوطء قيد به؛ لأنه لو قتلها غيره لا يسقط به المهر اتفاقاً، ولو قتلت هي نفسها، الصحيح أنه لا يسقط، ووجهه أن المهر لمولاها و لم يوجد منه منع، وكذا لو قتلت الحرة نفسها قبل الوطء لا يسقط، وقوله: "أمته" أي التي لم تكن مأذونة من المولى بالنكاح، فإن كانت مأذونة لم يسقط اتفاقاً، وقيد بالأمة ولو مدبرة؛ لأن المكاتبة لو قتلها سيدها لا يسقط اتفاقاً، وقوله: "قبل الوطء" ينبغي أن الخلوة تكون هنا كالوطء، وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : لا يسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته اعتباراً بموتها حتف أنفها؛ لأن المقتول ميت بأجله، والقتل موت فصار كما إذا قتلها أجنبي، ولأبي حنيفة: أن المعقود عليه فات قبل الدخول بفعل من له المهر وهو المولى، فلا يجب على الزوج، ولأن القتل جعل إتلافاً في حق أحكام الدنيا حتى يجب القصاص والدية والحرمان من الإرث، فكذا في المهر ، وبعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً، والمراد بالسيد العاقل البالغ حتى لو كان صبياً أو مجنوناً قالوا: يجب أن لا يسقط في قول أبي حنيفة. (فتح) لا بقتل الحرة إلخ عندنا؛ لأن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة أصلاً، ولهذا إذا قتل نفسه يغسل ويصلى عليه، ووجه آخر أن وهو قتل الحرة نفسها لو اعتبر تفويتاً للمهر إنما يكون تفويتاً بعد موتها، وبالموت ينتقل المهر إلى ورثتها فلا يسقط؛ لأنه للورثة لا لها، بخلاف قتل المولى أمته؛ لأن المهر للمولى، فكان مفوتاً حق نفسه
The mahr drops by the master killing his slave woman before intercourse. This is qualified: if someone else kills her, the mahr does not drop by consensus. If she kills herself, the correct position is that it does not drop, because the mahr belongs to her master and no prevention came from him. Likewise, if a free woman kills herself before intercourse, it does not drop. His statement: 'his slave woman' means she who is not authorized by the master for marriage; if she was authorized, it does not drop by consensus. He specified 'slave woman' even if mudabbara (promised emancipation), because if a mukatabah is killed by her master, it does not drop by consensus. His statement: 'before intercourse' implies that seclusion (khalwah) here is like intercourse. All this according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Sahibayn) said: The mahr does not drop by the master killing his slave woman, considering her death as if she died naturally; because the killed person dies at his appointed term, and killing is death, so it becomes as if a stranger killed her. Abu Hanifa's proof: The object of the contract (the marriage) is lost before entry by the action of the one to whom the mahr belongs, i.e., the master, so it is not obligatory upon the husband. And because killing is considered destruction in worldly rulings, such that qisas, diyah, and disinheritance are obligatory, so similarly in mahr. After intercourse, it does not drop by consensus. By 'master' is meant the sane adult; if the killer is a minor or insane, they said: it should not drop according to Abu Hanifa. The free woman's killing herself etc. according to us: because a person's injury to himself is not considered at all; hence if he kills himself, he is washed and prayed over. Another aspect: if the free woman's killing herself were considered a forfeiture of mahr, it would only be forfeiture after her death, and upon death the mahr transfers to her heirs, so it does not drop because it is for the heirs, not for her. This is unlike the master killing his slave woman because the mahr belongs to the master, so he forfeited his own right.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the master killing his slave woman on the mahr owed by her husband. According to Abu Hanifa, if the master kills his slave woman (who is not authorized to marry) before consummation of her marriage, the mahr is dropped from the husband. This is because the subject matter of the marriage (the slave woman's availability) is lost due to the action of the very person to whom the mahr would be paid (the master), so it would be unjust to require the husband to pay. The Sahibayn disagree: the mahr does not drop because the killing is treated like natural death; the slave woman died, and the mahr was incurred at the contract, so it remains due. The text also clarifies conditions: if someone else kills her, mahr does not drop; if she kills herself, it does not drop; if the master is a minor or insane, it should not drop; after consummation, it does not drop by consensus. The reason a free woman's suicide does not drop mahr is that the mahr would belong to her heirs, and her own act cannot forfeit their right.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The obligatory dower or bride-price that the husband must pay to the wife (or her master in case of a slave) upon marriage.
تفويت (tafwit): Forfeiture, loss of a right due to one's own action. Here, the master forfeits his right to mahr by destroying the slave woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which of the following cases does the mahr drop by consensus?
A) A master kills his authorized slave woman before consummation.
B) A master kills his unpermitted slave woman after consummation.
C) A stranger kills the slave woman before consummation.
D) A master kills his unpermitted slave woman before consummation, according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
The drop of mahr is not absolute; it is conditioned on the master being the killer, the slave being unpermitted, and the act occurring before consummation, and even then it is only according to Abu Hanifa's view, disagreed upon by the Sahibayn.

Dropping of Mahr When Master Kills Amah
Intra-Hanafi ruling on mahr when master kills amah before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mahr when a master kills his amah before consummation, and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's position, identifying the legal basis (dalil) for each.

Core Idea
When the master kills his own slave woman, he destroys his own right to the mahr, so it drops — but the Sahibayn analogize to natural death.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mahr (dower) when a master kills his own amah before consummation. The matn states that the mahr drops, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree, drawing an analogy to natural death. Understanding their arguments and the thamarah al-khilaf strengthens your ability to reason in marriage contracts with slave women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
Read the matn and commentary to extract the precise ruling: the mahr drops when the master kills his amah before consummation, provided the amah is not a mukataba or permitted to marry, and the master is a sane adult.
The ruling is conditional on the amah being unmarried and the master being legally responsible.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the master owns the mahr, and by killing the amah he destroys the subject of the contract before consummation, forfeiting his own right. Killing is considered destruction (itlaf) in worldly rulings, so it affects the mahr.
The key phrase: 'the subject of the contract was lost by the action of the one who owns the mahr'.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
Examine the Sahibayn's analogy: a killed person dies by his appointed term; killing is merely a form of death. Therefore, it is like natural death, and the mahr does not drop, just as if an outsider killed her.
Their qiyas (analogy) treats killing as equivalent to natural death in this context.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the husband pays no mahr to the master's estate; under the Sahibayn, the husband must still pay the mahr to the master's heirs, because the right is not forfeited.
This affects the financial obligations of the husband after the woman's death.
5
Review additional clarifications
The passage also clarifies that if the amah kills herself, the mahr does not drop; if an outsider kills her, it does not drop unanimously; and after consummation, the mahr never drops by consensus.
These edge cases reinforce the principle that only the master's own act before consummation triggers the drop.

Source Text

ويسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته قبل الوطء س م لا بقتل الحرة نفسها أي لا يسقط المهر وإن لم يرضيا عن ذمة الزوج وهو مكلف أي قبل وطي زوجها وله إجبارهما : [ أي العبد والأمة، ومعنى الإجبار أن ينفذ النكاح عليهما بغير رضائهما. (ع)]... ويسقط المهر بقتل السيد قيد به؛ لأنه لو قتلها غيره لا يسقط به المهر اتفاقاً، ولو قتلت هي نفسها، الصحيح أنه لا يسقط، ووجهه أن المهر لمولاها ولم يوجد منه منع، وكذا لو قتلت الحرة نفسها قبل الوطء لا يسقط، وقوله: "أمته" أي التي لم تكن مأذونة من المولى بالنكاح، فإن كانت مأذونة لم يسقط اتفاقاً، وقيد بالأمة ولو مدبرة؛ لأن المكاتبة لو قتلها سيدها لا يسقط اتفاقاً، وقوله: "قبل الوطء" ينبغي أن الخلوة تكون هنا كالوطء، وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : لا يسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته اعتباراً بموتها حتف أنفها؛ لأن المقتول ميت بأجله، والقتل موت فصار كما إذا قتلها أجنبي، ولأبي حنيفة: أن المعقود عليه فات قبل الدخول بفعل من له المهر وهو المولى، فلا يجب على الزوج، ولأن القتل جعل إتلافاً في حق أحكام الدنيا حتى يجب القصاص والدية والحرمان من الإرث، فكذا في المهر، وبعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً، والمراد بالسيد العاقل البالغ حتى لو كان صبياً أو مجنوناً قالوا: يجب أن لا يسقط في قول أبي حنيفة. (فتح) لا بقتل الحرة إلخ عندنا؛ لأن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة أصلاً، ولهذا إذا قتل نفسه يغسل ويصلى عليه، ووجه آخر أن وهو قتل الحرة نفسها لو اعتبر تفويتاً للمهر إنما يكون تفويتاً بعد موتها، وبالموت ينتقل المهر إلى ورثتها فلا يسقط؛ لأنه للورثة لا لها، بخلاف قتل المولى أمته؛ لأن المهر للمولى، فكان مفوتاً حق نفسه، قبله : أي قبل الوطء، وبعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً؛ لأن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة.
The dowry drops by the master killing his slave woman before intercourse. [Commentary:] He said: no, by the killing of the free woman herself, meaning the dowry does not drop. And if they do not consent from the responsibility of the husband, and he is legally responsible, i.e., before the intercourse of her husband. And he has the power to compel both of them: [i.e., the male slave and the female slave, ...] And the dowry drops by the master killing -- qualified by that; because if someone else killed her, the dowry does not drop by it unanimously. And if she killed herself, the correct view is that it does not drop, and its reason is that the dowry is for her master, and no prevention came from him. Similarly, if a free woman killed herself before intercourse, it does not drop. And his saying: 'his slave woman' means she who was not permitted by the master to marry; if she was permitted, it does not drop unanimously. And it is qualified as slave woman even if mudabbara; because if the master killed a mukataba, it does not drop unanimously. And his saying: 'before intercourse' it is fitting that privacy (khalwa) here is like intercourse. This is all according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: The dowry does not drop by the master killing his slave woman, analogizing to her dying a natural death; because the killed one dies by his appointed term, and killing is death, so it becomes as if an outsider killed her. For Abu Hanifa: The subject of the contract was lost before consummation by the action of the one who owns the dowry, which is the master, so it is not obligatory upon the husband. And because killing is considered destruction in the rulings of the world such that retaliation and blood money and denial of inheritance become obligatory, so likewise regarding the dowry. And after intercourse it does not drop by consensus. And what is meant by 'master' is the sane adult; if he was a child or insane, they said: it should not drop according to Abu Hanifa. (Fath) No, by the killing of the free woman etc., according to us; because a person's aggression against himself is not considered at all, and therefore if he kills himself, he is washed and prayed over. Another point: if the free woman killing herself were considered a loss of the dowry, the loss would only occur after her death, and by death the dowry transfers to her heirs, so it does not drop because it is for the heirs, not for her, unlike the master killing his slave woman because the dowry is for the master, so he is destroying his own right. Before him: i.e., before intercourse; after intercourse it does not drop by consensus because a person's aggression against himself is not considered.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute and cross-madhhab implications regarding the dowry (mahr) when a master kills his own slave woman (amah) before consummation. The matn states that the dowry drops. The commentary first clarifies that if someone else kills her, the dowry does not drop by consensus. If she kills herself, the correct view is it does not drop because the dowry belongs to her master. For a free woman, her suicide before intercourse does not drop the dowry. The slave woman is qualified as unmarried and not a mukataba. The ruling is limited to Abu Hanifa's opinion; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) hold that the dowry does not drop, analogizing to natural death, because the killed person dies by his term, and killing is just a form of death. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the subject of the contract (the mahr) was lost by the action of the one who owns it (the master), so the husband is not obligated. Additionally, killing is considered destruction in worldly rulings, so it triggers retaliation, blood money, and disinheritance; similarly, the dowry is lost. After consummation, it does not drop by consensus. The master must be a sane adult; if he is a child or insane, the dowry should not drop. For the free woman killing herself, the key principle is that a person's aggression against oneself is not considered in worldly rulings; thus the dowry does not drop.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift owed by the husband to the wife, but in this context, it is owed to the master of the slave woman.
حتف أنفها (hatf anfiha): Natural death by no external cause.

Permission for 'Azl with a Slave Woman: Master vs. Her Consent
Ruling on whose permission is needed for 'azl with a married slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on permission for 'azl with a slave woman, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
For a married slave, the child belongs to the master, so his permission is needed for 'azl.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about coitus interruptus ('azl) with a slave woman who is married. The core issue is whose consent is required: the master's or the slave woman's? The text explains Abu Hanifa's view, the objection of his two disciples, and the evidence from a hadith. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the underlying legal logic (ta'lil) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal dispute
The act is 'azl (coitus interruptus) with a married slave woman. The dispute is about whose permission is necessary for it to be permissible.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the master's permission is required because the primary purpose of marrying a slave is to produce a child, which belongs to the master. The slave woman has no right to sexual satisfaction or to demand marriage.
His argument is based on the ownership of the child (right of the master) and the limited rights of a slave woman in marriage.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the slave woman's permission is required because marriage is meant for mutual satisfaction and protection from fornication, and 'azl undermines that. They analogize to a free woman.
Their argument emphasizes the purpose of marriage (shari'a purpose) and the woman's interest in fulfillment.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa responds that a slave woman's marriage is not for her own right; she cannot even demand to be married. The real purpose is the child, which is the master's right, unlike a free woman where both parents share custody rights.
This response distinguishes between the rights of a free woman (haq al-hadana) and a slave woman (no such right).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the relied-upon view
Under Abu Hanifa, the husband may practice 'azl only with the master's consent. Under the Sahibayn, he needs the slave woman's consent. The text sides with Abu Hanifa, and this is the mufta bihi position.
The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri is cited to support permissibility in general, but does not directly resolve the dispute over whose consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإذن في العزل: [وهو الإنزال خارج الفرج في الجماع. (ط، ع)] إنما كان الإذن في العزل عن الأمة المنكوحة للمولى؛ لأنه منع عن حدوث الولد وهو حق المولى، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما أن الإذن إليها؛ لأن النكاح شرع صيانة لها عن السفاح وذا إنما يكون إذا كان كل واحد منهما قاضيا لشهوته والعزل يخل به فشرط فيه رضاها كما في الحرة وله أن الأمة لا حق لها في قضاء الشهوة؛ لأن النكاح لم يشرع حقا لها ابتداء وبقاء، فإنها لا تتمكن من مطالبة سيدها بالتزويج، والعزل يخل بالمقصود وهو الولد وهو حق المولى لا حق الأمة، بخلاف الحرة؛ لأن في الحرة المنكوحة الإذن إليها في العزل لتعلق حقهما بالولد وهو حق الحضانة، فالإذن إليها إجماعا. ثم لا اختلاف في جوازه في الأمة المملوكة لما روي عن أبي سعيد الخدري الله أن رجلا أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: إن لي جارية وأنا أعزل عنها وأكره أن تحمل وإن اليهود تحدث أن العزل الموءودة الصغرى، قال: "كذبت يهود لو أراد الله أن يخلقه ما استطعت أن تصرفه ثم العزل مباح برضا المرأة الحرة أو برضا مولى امرأته الأمة، وفي الأمة المملوكة بغير رضاها. (فتح)
And permission for 'azl: [It is ejaculation outside the vulva during intercourse. (Tahaawi, Ayni)] Permission for 'azl from the married slave woman belongs to the master; because it prevents the occurrence of a child, and that is the right of the master. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), permission belongs to her; because marriage is legislated as a protection for her from fornication, and that is only when each of them satisfies his desire, and 'azl contradicts that, so her consent is required as with a free woman. And for him (Abu Hanifa): the slave woman has no right in the satisfaction of desire; because marriage was not legislated as a right for her initially or as a continuation, for she is not able to demand from her master that he marry her off. And 'azl contradicts the intended purpose, which is the child, and that is the right of the master, not the right of the slave woman. Unlike the free woman; because for the free married woman, permission for 'azl belongs to her due to the connection of both their rights to the child, and that is the right of custody, so permission belongs to her by consensus. Then there is no dispute about its permissibility regarding the owned slave woman, based on what was narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him) that a man came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said: 'I have a slave girl and I practice 'azl with her, and I dislike that she becomes pregnant, and the Jews say that 'azl is minor infanticide.' He said: 'The Jews lied. If Allah wanted to create it, you would not be able to repel it.' Then 'azl is permitted with the consent of a free woman or with the consent of the master of his slave wife, and regarding the owned slave woman, even without her consent. (Fath)
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding permission for 'azl (coitus interruptus) with a slave woman. The text first defines 'azl. Then it states that according to Abu Hanifa, permission rests with the master because 'azl prevents the birth of a child, which is the master's right. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that permission belongs to the slave woman herself because marriage is meant to satisfy both parties' desires and protect from fornication, and 'azl impedes that. Abu Hanifa's response is that the slave woman has no right to satisfaction of desire; marriage is not legislated as her right initially or continuously — she cannot even demand to be married off. The real purpose of marring a slave is the child, which is the master's right, unlike a free woman where both parents have a right to the child (especially custody) so her consent is needed by consensus. The text then cites a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: a man asked the Prophet about 'azl with his slave girl, and the Prophet declared it permissible, rejecting the Jewish claim that it is minor infanticide. The final ruling: 'azl is permissible with the consent of a free woman or the consent of the master of his slave wife. For an owned slave woman, it is permissible even without her consent.

Key Terms
عزل ('azl): Ejaculation outside the vulva during intercourse to prevent pregnancy.
موءودة صغرى (maw'udah sughra): Minor infanticide — the Jewish claim that 'azl is akin to burying a child alive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the purpose of marrying the slave is the child, which belongs to the master
C) Because the master has the right to satisfy his desire with her
D) Because the Prophet explicitly said so

Key Takeaway
Permission for 'azl with a married slave woman belongs to the master because the child is his right; the slave woman's consent is not required.

The Option of a Mukatabah Upon Emancipation
Inter-madhhab dispute on khiyar for mukatabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the option for a mukatabah upon emancipation and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of Barira.

Core Idea
A mukatabah gets the option upon emancipation because Barira's hadith establishes it and ownership increases over her.

This lesson examines a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school regarding whether a female slave who has a kitaba contract (mukatabah) receives the option (khiyar) to annul or keep her marriage upon emancipation. The Hanafis argue that the hadith of Barira explicitly proves this right, while the Shafi'is contend that her initial consent eliminates the need for an option. Understanding this ikhtilaf deepens your appreciation of how texts are reconciled and how legal rulings are derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State clearly what the Hanafi school says: a mukatabah (and similarly an umm walad) has the option to annul or keep her marriage upon emancipation, even if her husband is free.
The option must be exercised in the same council where she learns of the emancipation.
2
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that the mukatabah does not have the option because her marriage required her consent initially, making her like a free woman without need for a post-emancipation choice.
They also cite a version of the Barira hadith where her husband was a slave to argue the option is only for that case.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis rely on the hadith of Barira, who was a mukatabah, and the fact that the Prophet gave her the option. Additionally, they use a second narration where Barira's husband was free, reconciling both to establish the option universally.
The argument also includes a qiyas: ownership increases over her like a slave, which necessitates the option.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the mukatabah can annul the marriage; under Shafi'i, the marriage continues without an option.
This difference affects the rights of the woman and the status of the marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعتقت أمة أو مكاتبة خيرت ولو زوجها حرا، ولو نكحت بلا إذن من المولى أو أم ولد أي كان زوجها وعند زفر والشافعي: يسقط اعتباراً بالردة وبقتل المولى أمته ولنا كما سبق أن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة في أحكام الدنيا فشابه موتها حتف أنفها وقتل المولى معتبر في أحكام الدنيا حتى وجبت الكفارة عليه، والخلاف مقيد بما إذا لم تكن مستحقة للقتل، فلو كانت مستحقة له فلا مهر لها إجماعاً، ولو قتلت الحرة نفسها بعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً. (فتح) مكاتبة: ولو حكما كمعتقة البعض والحال أنهما قد تزوجتا. (ع) خيرت كل واحدة منهما في مجلس علما بالعتق بين إبقاء النكاح وفسخه. (ط) ولو زوجها حراً: [ولو صغيرةً تأخر خيارها إلى بلوغها، وليس لها خيار بلوغ في الأصح، فإن اختارت نفسها فلا مهر لها أو زوجها فالمهر لسيدها. (ط)] وقال الشافعي: لا خيار للمكاتبة؛ لأنه لا نفاذ للنكاح إلا برضاها فصارت كالحرة، ولنا: ما روي من حديث بريرة وكانت مكاتبة؛ ولأن الملك يزداد عليها كالأمة وهو الموجب، وقال أيضاً: لا خيار لها إن كان زوجها حراً؛ لحديث بريرة من رواية عائشة لما أن النبي الله . خيرها وكان زوجها عبداً، رواه مسلم. ولنا: حديث عائشة ما أن زوج بريرة كان حراً حين أعتقت رواه البخاري ومسلم، وحديثنا أولى؛ لكونه مثبتاً للحرية لاتفاقهم أنه كان قبل ذلك عبداً أو نقول: ليس فيما روي دلالة على أنه إذا كان حراً لا يكون لها الخيار فلا يمكن الاحتجاج به إلا على ثبوت الخيار لها فيما إذا كان زوجها عبداً، ونحن نقول بموجبه وبموجب الحديث الآخر جمعاً بين الحديثين، وزفر يخالفنا في المكاتبة كالشافعي، والحجة عليه حديث بريرة وكانت مكاتبة. رمز الحقائق (۲۲۱/۱] (فتح) ولو نكحت أمة أو مدبرة أو أم ولد أو مكاتبة. (ط)
And if a slave woman or mukatabah is emancipated, she is given a choice, even if her husband is free. And if she marries without permission from the master, or (the case of) the umm walad, i.e., her husband was (a slave). According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: it drops (the mahr?) based on apostasy and the master killing his slave woman. And for us, as before, a person's aggression against himself is not considered in the rulings of this world, so it resembles her dying a natural death, and the killing by the master is considered in the rulings of the world until expiation is obligatory on him. The dispute is restricted to when she was not deserving of execution; if she was deserving, there is no mahr by consensus. And if a free woman kills herself after intercourse, it does not drop by consensus. (Fath) Mukatabah: even if it is a ruling like one partially free, while they have married. (Ayni) Each of them is given a choice in the council upon learning of the emancipation between keeping the marriage and annulling it. (Tahaawi) And if her husband is free: [If she was a minor, her choice is delayed until she reaches puberty, and there is no option of puberty according to the most correct view. If she chooses herself, there is no mahr for her, or if her husband married her, the mahr is for her master. (Tahaawi)] And al-Shafi'i said: There is no option for the mukatabah; because the marriage has no effect except with her consent, so she becomes like a free woman. And for us: What was narrated from the hadith of Barira and she was a mukatabah; and because ownership increases over her like the slave woman, and that is the cause. He also said: She also has no option if her husband is free; based on the hadith of Barira narrated by Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave her a choice and her husband was a slave, reported by Muslim. And for us: The hadith of Aisha that Barira's husband was free when she was emancipated, reported by Bukhari and Muslim. And our hadith is more preferable because it establishes freedom, and they all agreed that before that he was a slave. Or we say: There is no indication in what was narrated that if he is free there is no option for her, so it cannot be used as proof except for the establishment of option for her when her husband is a slave, and we accept its implication and the implication of the other hadith to combine both hadiths. And Zufar disagrees with us regarding the mukatabah like al-Shafi'i, and the proof against him is the hadith of Barira and she was a mukatabah. Ramz al-Haqa'iq (1/221) (Fath) And if a slave woman, or a mudabbara, or an umm walad, or a mukatabah married (without permission). (Tahaawi)
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with dispute regarding the option (khiyar) given to a slave woman (amah), mukatabah, or umm walad upon emancipation. The matn states: if a slave woman or mukatabah is freed, she is given a choice (to keep or annul her marriage), even if her husband is free. The commentary explains that 'mukatabah' includes one partially free. The option must be exercised in the council where she learns of the emancipation. If she was a minor, the option is delayed until puberty. If she chooses to keep the marriage, the mahr belongs to her master; if she chooses herself (annulment), she gets no mahr. The passage then presents the Shafi'i objection: the mukatabah has no option because her consent was already required for the marriage, like a free woman. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith of Barira, who was a mukatabah and was given the option. Additionally, ownership over her increases (like a slave), which is the cause for the option. Another objection from Shafi'is is that if the husband is free, she has no option, citing a version of the hadith. The Hanafis respond with another version of the hadith stating Barira's husband was free, and they reconcile the narrations. Zufar agrees with Shafi'i against the Hanafis on the mukatabah. The proof against him is the hadith of Barira, which explicitly involves a mukatabah. The final line mentions that if a slave woman, mudabbara, umm walad, or mukatabah marries without permission, the ruling applies.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The option to either keep or annul the marriage upon emancipation.
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A female slave who has a contract of kitaba with her master to earn her freedom by paying installments.
عتق (itq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, why does a mukatabah have the option upon emancipation?
A) Because she is like a free woman and can decide her marriage
B) Because the hadith of Barira proves it
C) Because her master chooses for her
D) Because the marriage contract was invalid initially

Key Takeaway
The mukatabah's option upon emancipation is established by the hadith of Barira, and the Hanafis reconcile contradictory narrations to uphold the option regardless of the husband's status.

Master Killing His Enslaved Woman Before Consummation: Does the Mahr Drop?
Dispute on whether mahr drops if master kills his enslaved woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that mahr does not drop when a master kills his own enslaved woman before consummation, and evaluate the Zufar/Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Self-injury is not considered in worldly rulings; mahr remains despite the master's killing.

This passage addresses a nuanced dispute: if a master kills his own enslaved woman before consummation, does the mahr (bridal gift) drop? Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue yes by analogy, but the Hanafi school insists the mahr remains due, based on a foundational principle about self-injury. Understanding this case reveals how Hanafis prioritize established usul principles over apparent analogies.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the mahr does not drop; it remains an obligation on the master (or his estate).
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the mahr drops entirely.
They analogize to two cases: apostasy (where mahr drops) and a master killing his enslaved woman (where the marriage terminates without mahr).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine their analogical reasoning: apostasy voids the marriage and mahr; killing by the master also terminates the marriage, so mahr should drop.
Their qiyas is based on apparent similarity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal relies on the principle that a person's injury to himself (or here, injury to his own property) is not legally effective in worldly rulings. Thus, the master's killing is treated like a natural death for mahr purposes.
The Hanafi response also distinguishes the two analogies: apostasy is an act of the slave herself, and the master-killing case involves intentional killing requiring expiation, which does not apply here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, the heirs of the enslaved woman receive the half mahr; under the opposing view, they get nothing.
This outcome depends on whether the principle of self-injury non-consideration is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند زفر والشافعي: يسقط اعتباراً بالردة وبقتل المولى أمته ولنا كما سبق أن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة في أحكام الدنيا فشابه موتها حتف أنفها وقتل المولى معتبر في أحكام الدنيا حتى وجبت الكفارة عليه، والخلاف مقيد بما إذا لم تكن مستحقة للقتل، فلو كانت مستحقة له فلا مهر لها إجماعاً، ولو قتلت الحرة نفسها بعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً. (فتح) قبله : أي قبل الوطء، وبعد الوطء لا يسقط إجماعاً؛ لأن جناية المرء على نفسه غير معتبرة.
And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: it drops, by analogy with apostasy and with the master killing his enslaved woman. And our proof is as before that a person's injury to himself is not considered in worldly rulings, so it resembles her dying a natural death, and the master's killing is considered in worldly rulings such that expiation is obligatory upon him. The disagreement is restricted to when she was not deserving of execution. If she was deserving, then there is no mahr for her by consensus. And if a free woman kills herself after consummation, it does not drop by consensus. (Fath) Before it: i.e., before consummation, and after consummation it does not drop by consensus because a person's injury to himself is not considered.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute about the effect of a master killing his own enslaved woman before consummation of her marriage. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the mahr drops, analogizing to apostasy (where the marriage is voided) and to the master killing his enslaved woman (where the mahr is not due). The Hanafi response, based on the principle that a person's self-injury has no legal effect in worldly matters, argues that the enslaved woman's death at the master's hands is like a natural death for the purpose of mahr, so the mahr remains due. However, if the enslaved woman was lawfully sentenced to death (e.g., for apostasy or murder), then by consensus no mahr is due. The commentary then clarifies that the word 'before it' refers to before consummation; after consummation, the mahr does not drop by consensus because the full mahr is established.

Key Terms
جناية المرء على نفسه (jināyat al-marʾ ʿalā nafsihi): A person's harmful act against their own person (e.g., suicide or self-injury); in Hanafi law, such acts are not considered legally effective in worldly matters (ahkām al-dunyā).
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift (dower) that becomes obligatory upon the husband by virtue of the marriage contract, and half is due before consummation, full after.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master kills his enslaved woman before consummation, the mahr:
A) drops because of analogy with apostasy.
B) does not drop because self-injury is not considered in worldly rulings.
C) drops only if she was lawfully sentenced to death.
D) does not drop because of consensus among all schools.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the master's killing of his own enslaved woman does not cause the mahr to fall, because self-injury has no effect on worldly financial obligations.

Permission for 'Azl with a Married Enslaved Woman: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on permission for 'azl with enslaved wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn on permission for 'azl with a married enslaved woman and apply the Hanafi tarjih mechanism.

Core Idea
Permission for 'azl with a married enslaved woman requires the master's consent (Abu Hanifa) because offspring belong to the master, not the slave.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding coitus interruptus ('azl) with a married enslaved woman. While both sides agree on the permissibility of 'azl in principle, they differ on whose consent is necessary—the master or the enslaved woman herself. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists weigh competing rights: the master's right to offspring versus the enslaved woman's right to marital fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested act and its ruling
What is 'azl? What is the baseline ruling (mubah)? Then identify the point of dispute: who must give permission for 'azl with a married enslaved woman.
The passage focuses on permission, not the permissibility of 'azl itself, which is agreed upon.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa requires the master's permission. His proof: offspring is the master's right; the enslaved woman has no right to sexual satisfaction because she cannot demand marriage.
He uses a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) based on the nature of slave ownership.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn require the enslaved woman's consent, analogizing to a free woman. Their proof: marriage protects her from fornication, and 'azl disrupts fulfillment.
They apply qiyas jali (obvious analogy) to the case of a free woman.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the tarjih
The practical difference: whose consent binds the husband? The passage presents Abu Hanifa's view with stronger reasoning, but no explicit tarjih is given.
The later Hanafi tradition may prefer Abu Hanifa's view, but both are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإذن في العزل: [وهو الإنزال خارج الفرج في الجماع. (ط، ع)] إنما كان الإذن في العزل عن الأمة المنكوحة للمولى؛ لأنه منع عن حدوث الولد وهو حق المولى، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما أن الإذن إليها؛ لأن النكاح شرع صيانة لها عن السفاح وذا إنما يكون إذا كان كل واحد منهما قاضيا لشهوته والعزل يخل به فشرط فيه رضاها كما في الحرة وله أن الأمة لا حق لها في قضاء الشهوة؛ لأن النكاح لم يشرع حقا لها ابتداء وبقاء، فإنها لا تتمكن من مطالبة سيدها بالتزويج، والعزل يخل بالمقصود وهو الولد وهو حق المولى لا حق الأمة، بخلاف الحرة؛ لأن في الحرة المنكوحة الإذن إليها في العزل لتعلق حقهما بالولد وهو حق الحضانة، فالإذن إليها إجماعا. ثم لا اختلاف في جوازه في الأمة المملوكة لما روي عن أبي سعيد الخدري الله أن رجلا أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: إن لي جارية وأنا أعزل عنها وأكره أن تحمل وإن اليهود تحدث أن العزل الموءودة الصغرى، قال: "كذبت يهود لو أراد الله أن يخلقه ما استطعت أن تصرفه ثم العزل مباح برضا المرأة الحرة أو برضا مولى امرأته الأمة، وفي الأمة المملوكة بغير رضاها. (فتح)
And permission for 'azl: [i.e., ejaculating outside the vagina during intercourse. (T, A)] Permission for 'azl from the married enslaved woman is given to the master because it prevents the occurrence of a child, which is the right of the master. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), permission is given to her because marriage is legislated for her protection from fornication, and that only happens when each of them fulfills his desire, and 'azl disrupts that, so her consent is required as in the case of a free woman. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the enslaved woman has no right to the fulfillment of desire because marriage was not legislated as a right for her initially or continuously; she cannot demand her master to marry her off. And 'azl disrupts the purpose, which is the child, and that is the right of the master, not the right of the enslaved woman. Unlike the free woman, because for a free married woman, permission for 'azl is given to her because both have a right to the child, which is the right of custody, so permission is given to her by consensus. Then there is no disagreement about its permissibility with a owned (unmarried) enslaved woman, due to what is narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that a man came to the Prophet and said: 'I have a slave girl and I practice 'azl with her and dislike her becoming pregnant, and the Jews say that 'azl is minor infanticide.' He said: 'The Jews lied. If Allah intended to create it, you could not turn it away.' Then 'azl is permissible with the consent of a free woman or with the consent of the master of his women who is an enslaved woman (i.e., his wife), and with a owned enslaved woman without her consent. (Fath)
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses permission for coitus interruptus ('azl) with an enslaved woman who is married to a free man. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, permission must be obtained from the enslaved woman's master, because the primary purpose of marriage with an enslaved woman is to produce offspring, which is the master's right (as the children of an enslaved woman belong to the master). The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that permission must be from the enslaved woman herself, analogizing to a free woman, because marriage is meant to satisfy her desire and 'azl would deprive her of that satisfaction. The text provides Abu Hanifa's reasoning that an enslaved woman has no inherent right to marital fulfillment; she cannot even demand marriage from her master. For a free woman, permission is given to her by consensus because both spouses have a right to the child (the right of custody). The passage then clarifies that there is no disagreement that 'azl is permissible with an unmarried (owned) enslaved woman without her consent, citing a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that 'azl is not infanticide.

Key Terms
عزل (ʿazl): Coitus interruptus, i.e., withdrawing before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.
مولى (mawlā): Master or owner of a slave; in this context, the owner of the enslaved woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, permission for 'azl with a married enslaved woman is given to:
A) The master
B) The enslaved woman
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The permission for 'azl with a married enslaved woman is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa requires the master's consent, while the Sahibayn require the enslaved woman's consent, based on differing views of whose right to offspring and fulfillment prevails.
