Option for a Mukataba Upon Manumission
Option for a mukataba or partial slave to choose between annulment or keeping marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mukataba has the option upon manumission according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A mukataba upon manumission retains the right to choose her marriage—just as Barira did.

The Hanafi school extends the option of manumission to a mukataba (a slave under a contract of manumission), granting her the choice to keep or annul her marriage upon becoming free. This ruling, grounded in the hadith of Barira and the principle of increased ownership, is contested by Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar, who restrict the option to ordinary slaves or to cases where the husband is a slave. This lesson unpacks the evidence and reconciliation of conflicting narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine that the act is the right (khiyar) of a mukataba upon manumission to choose between keeping or annulling her marriage. The ruling is that she has this option unconditionally according to the Hanafi school.
The option must be exercised in the same gathering where she learns of the manumission, unless she is a minor.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof
The primary proof is the hadith of Barira, who was a mukataba and was given the choice upon manumission. Additionally, the effective cause (illah) is the increase in ownership (malik) over herself, analogous to an ordinary enslave woman upon manumission.
The hadith is narrated by 'Aisha in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that a mukataba has no option because the marriage was concluded with her consent, equating her to a free woman. They also point to a narration of Barira where her husband was a slave, implying the option only exists if the husband is a slave.
Zufar is an early Hanafi jurist who sided with al-Shafi'i on this issue.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters with another authentic narration from 'Aisha stating that Barira's husband was free at the time of manumission. They reconcile the two hadiths by affirming the option in both cases, using the general principle that the option is not conditional on the husband's status. The increase in ownership is the operative cause.
The Hanafi response demonstrates reconciliation of conflicting narrations (jama').
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Hanafi view, a mukataba may leave her free husband; under the Shafi'i view, she cannot and the marriage continues. This affects the validity of subsequent marriages and mahr obligations.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكاتبة: ولو حكما كمعتقة البعض والحال أنهما قد تزوجتا. (ع) خيرت كل واحدة منهما في مجلس علما بالعتق بين إبقاء النكاح وفسخه. (ط) ولو زوجها حراً: [ولو صغيرةً تأخر خيارها إلى بلوغها، وليس لها خيار بلوغ في الأصح، فإن اختارت نفسها فلا مهر لها أو زوجها فالمهر لسيدها. (ط)] وقال الشافعي: لا خيار للمكاتبة؛ لأنه لا نفاذ للنكاح إلا برضاها فصارت كالحرة، ولنا: ما روي من حديث بريرة وكانت مكاتبة؛ ولأن الملك يزداد عليها كالأمة وهو الموجب، وقال أيضاً: لا خيار لها إن كان زوجها حراً؛ لحديث بريرة من رواية عائشة لما أن النبي الله . خيرها وكان زوجها عبداً، رواه مسلم. ولنا: حديث عائشة ما أن زوج بريرة كان حراً حين أعتقت رواه البخاري ومسلم، وحديثنا أولى؛ لكونه مثبتاً للحرية لاتفاقهم أنه كان قبل ذلك عبداً أو نقول: ليس فيما روي دلالة على أنه إذا كان حراً لا يكون لها الخيار فلا يمكن الاحتجاج به إلا على ثبوت الخيار لها فيما إذا كان زوجها عبداً، ونحن نقول بموجبه وبموجب الحديث الآخر جمعاً بين الحديثين، وزفر يخالفنا في المكاتبة كالشافعي، والحجة عليه حديث بريرة وكانت مكاتبة. رمز الحقائق (۲۲۱/۱] (فتح)
Mukataba: even if by legal fiction like one who is partially free, while they have married. (A) Each one of them is given a choice in the gathering where they learn of the manumission, between keeping the marriage or dissolving it. (T) And if a free man married her: [Even if she is a minor, her choice is delayed until she reaches majority, and she does not have the option of puberty in the correct view. If she chooses herself, then no mahr for her; or if her husband chooses, the mahr is for her master. (T)] And al-Shafi'i said: No option for the mukataba because the marriage was only valid with her consent, so she is like a free woman. Our proof: what is narrated from the hadith of Barira, and she was a mukataba; and because ownership increases over her like an enslaved woman, which is the effective cause. He also said: She has no option if her husband is free, based on the hadith of Barira from the narration of 'Aisha when the Prophet gave her the choice and her husband was a slave, narrated by Muslim. Our proof: the hadith of 'Aisha that Barira's husband was free when she was manumitted, narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. Our hadith is stronger because it affirms free status, and they all agreed he was a slave before. Or we say: There is no indication in what is narrated that if he is free she has no option; so it can only be used to prove the option when the husband is a slave. We accept the implication of both hadiths to reconcile them. Zufar disagrees with us about the mukataba like al-Shafi'i, and the proof against him is the hadith of Barira, and she was a mukataba. Ramz al-Haqa'iq (221/1) (Fath)
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage addresses the option (khiyar) of a mukataba (a slave who has contracted to purchase her freedom) or a partial slave (e.g., one who is part free) upon being manumitted while married. According to the Hanafi school, such a woman has the option to either retain the marriage or annul it, and this choice must be made in the same gathering where she learns of the manumission. If she was married to a free man, and she is a minor, the option is deferred until she reaches majority, but she does not have the option of puberty (i.e., when she reaches majority she does not get a new option). The text then presents the Shafi'i objection that because the marriage was concluded with her consent, she is like a free woman and thus no option upon manumission. The Hanafi response cites the hadith of Barira, who was a mukataba and was given the choice. Additionally, ownership (malik) increases upon her after manumission (since she becomes owner of herself), which is the effective cause for the option, just like an enslaved woman. The Shafi'i also argues that if her husband is free, she has no option based on a version of Barira's story. The Hanafis counter with another authentic narration that Barira's husband was free at the time of manumission, and reconcile the hadiths by saying that the option is established whether the husband is free or slave. Zufar agrees with al-Shafi'i on the mukataba issue.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātaba): A female slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with her master, whereby she will pay a sum to earn her freedom.
خيار (khiyār): Option or choice; here specifically the option a female slave has upon manumission to either continue or annul her marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a mukataba who is manumitted while married?
A) She must remain married.
B) She has the option to annul the marriage or keep it.
C) The marriage is automatically annulled.
D) The option exists only if her husband was a slave.

Key Takeaway
Upon manumission, a mukataba has the option (khiyar) to either keep or annul her marriage, regardless of her husband's status, based on the hadith of Barira and the principle of increased ownership.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage of an Enslaved Woman Without Permission That Becomes Valid Upon Manumission
Ruling with proof using qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of an enslaved woman who marries without permission and is later manumitted, and apply the distinction between marriage and purchase in determining the contract's validity.

Core Idea
Marriage by an enslaved woman without permission becomes valid upon manumission; no option because she consented freely.

One of the more nuanced rulings in Hanafi fiqh deals with contracts entered by an enslaved person without the master's permission. This passage examines the case of an enslaved woman who marries without her master's consent and is later set free. The Hanafi school applies a sophisticated analysis: the contract is not void but merely obstructed by the master's right, which ceases upon manumission. You will see how the ruling is derived and how it differs from other contracts like purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the initial impediment
The marriage was contracted by an enslaved woman without her master's permission. Such a contract is not void ab initio but is affected by the master's right, which prevents immediate effectiveness.
2
Determine the effect of manumission
Once the woman is manumitted, the master's right ceases. The marriage becomes valid (nafadha) without needing a new consent or contract. The woman has no option to annul because she chose the husband freely.
The term 'nafadha' here means the contract takes effect retroactively or at that moment.
3
Distinguish from the purchase case
An objection is raised: if an enslaved woman buys something, the purchase is void upon manumission. The Hanafi response hinges on the nature of the right transferred: purchase gives ownership to the master, marriage gives a right to the woman herself. Thus manumission does not retroactively harm the master in marriage.
This is an application of istihsan over qiyas.
4
Determine the mahr based on timing of intercourse
If the husband had intercourse before manumission, the mahr belongs to the master because he benefited from the master's property. If after, it belongs to the woman.
An objection that two mahrs should apply (mahr al-mithl for intercourse before a valid marriage and the specified mahr) is resolved by istihsan: only the specified mahr is due, as the contract's enforcement relates back.
5
Examine the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue the marriage is void because manumission is not equivalent to the master's approval. The Hanafi position prevails by distinguishing between guardianship and ownership rights.
This ikhtilaf has practical implications for the validity of subsequent acts like inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نكحت أمة أو مدبرة أو أم ولد أو مكاتبة. (ط) فعتقت نفذ بلا خيار، فلو وطئ قبله فالمهر له وإلا لها، ومن وطئ أمة ابنه فولدت قبل إذنه النكاح عليها المسألة لها زوج الأمة أي للمولى أي للأمة ولو صغيرا أي الأمة فعتقت نفذ بلا خيار: عتقت بفتح المهملة مبنيا للفاعل ولا يجوز ضمه بالبناء للمفعول؛ لأنه لازم، وفرض في الأمة وإن كان العبد كذلك ليفرع مسألة الخيار ؛ لأنها تختص بالإماء دون العبيد، وصورة المسألة: أنه لو زوجت أمة بغير إذن مولاها ثم عتقت قبل رد المولى نكاحها صح النكاح؛ لأنها من أهل العبادة وكان امتناع النفوذ لحق المولى وقد زال حق المولى بإزالة الملك فينفذ ثم لا خيار لها؛ لأنها قد رضيت باختيارها إياه بلا جبر من المولى، فإن قيل: ينقض هذا بما إذا اشترت الأمة شيئاً ثم أعتقها المولى فإن الشراء يبطل؟ قلنا: إنما كان كذلك؛ لأن الشراء انعقد موجبا للملك للمولى حين اشترت فلو نفذ بعد عتقها كان موجبا للملك لها، فيتضرر من له الحق أي المولى، وأما هنا فانعقد العقد موجبا للحق لها فافترقا، ووجه عدم الخيار أيضاً أن نفوذ النكاح بعد العتق فلا يتصور ازدياد الملك عليها، وثبوت الخيار يكون باعتبار ازدياد الملك، وقال زفر والشافعي: بطل النكاح؛ لأنه كان موقوفا على إجازة المولى والإعتاق ليس بإجازة وبعد العتق ارتفعت ولايته فيبطل. (فتح، مستخلص) وطئ قبله أي قبل العتق فيما إذا تزوجت بغير إذن. (ع) فالمهر له وإلا لها أي لو وطئ الزوج هذه الأمة قبل عتقها فالمهر للمولى ؛ لاستيفائه المنافع المملوكة للمولى، وإن لم يطأها قبل العتق فالمهر للأمة المعتقة؛ لاستيفائه المنافع المملوكة لها، فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يجب مهران: مهر المثل بالدخول قبل إنفاذ النكاح بناء على أنه عقد موقوف ومهر بالنكاح وهو المسمى كما قال لأجنبية: إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق فتزوجها ودخل بها طلقت وعليه نصف المسمى في الطلاق قبل الدخول بحكم العقد ومهر بالدخول بعد الطلاق؟ قلنا: القياس هكذا، ولكن استحساناً أو جبنا مهراً واحداً وهو المسمى وقت العقد؛ لأن نفاذ العقد بالعتق يستند إلى وقت وجود العقد فصحت التسمية ووجب المسمى. (مستخلص) وإلا لها: أي وإن لم يطأها الزوج حتى أعتقها مولاها فالمهر لها.
And if an enslaved woman, or a mudabbara, or umm walad, or mukataba, married (without permission) (T), and then she is manumitted, the marriage becomes effective without option. If he had intercourse before (manumission), the mahr is for the master; otherwise for her. And whoever has intercourse with his son's enslaved woman and she gives birth before his permission... the issue: for her, the husband of the enslaved woman i.e., for the master, i.e., for the enslaved woman, even if he is young, i.e., the enslaved woman. Then she is manumitted and the marriage is effective without option: (the word 'manumitted' is pronounced with a fatha on the 'ta' as active voice, not passive, because it is intransitive. It is assumed in the case of an enslaved woman; though a male slave is similar, the issue of option is branched off because it is specific to female slaves, not male. The scenario: if an enslaved woman is married without her master's permission, then she is manumitted before the master repudiates her marriage, the marriage becomes valid because she is an eligible person for worship (i.e., mukallafa) and the lack of effect was due to the master's right, and his right has ceased by removal of ownership, so it takes effect, and then she has no option because she had chosen him freely without coercion from the master. If it is said: This is contradicted by the case where an enslaved woman buys something and then her master manumits her – the purchase is void. We say: That is because the purchase contract was concluded to give ownership to the master; if it took effect after her manumission, it would give ownership to her, harming the master. Here, the contract was concluded to give a right to her, so they differ. The reason for no option also is that the marriage becomes effective after manumission, so there is no increase of ownership over her (since she already owns herself), and the option is based on increase of ownership. Zufar and al-Shafi'i say: The marriage is void because it was suspended on the master's approval, and manumission is not approval, and after manumission his guardianship is lifted, so it becomes void. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'Intercourse before it' i.e., before manumission, when she married without permission. (A) So the mahr is for him i.e., for the master: if the husband had intercourse with this enslaved woman before her manumission, the mahr is for the master because he derived benefit from property owned by the master. If he did not have intercourse before manumission, the mahr is for the manumitted enslaved woman because he derived benefit from property owned by her. If it is said: Two mahrs should be obligatory: the mahr al-mithl for intercourse before the marriage's enforcement (since it was a suspended contract) and the mahr named in the contract (the specified mahr), similar to the case where a man says to a strange woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' then he marries her and has intercourse, she is divorced and half the specified mahr is due upon divorce before consummation by virtue of the contract, and a mahr for intercourse after divorce. We say: Qiyas would require that, but istihsan (juristic preference) we make one mahr obligatory, the one specified at the contract, because the enforcement of the contract by manumission relates back to the time of the contract, so the specification is valid and the specified mahr is due. (Mustakhlas) 'Otherwise for her': i.e., if the husband did not have intercourse until her master manumitted her, the mahr is for her.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage details the ruling on an enslaved woman who marries without her master's permission and is subsequently manumitted. According to the Hanafi school, the marriage becomes valid immediately upon manumission, without the need for the woman to exercise an option (khiyar). The reason is that the impediment to the marriage's validity was the master's right, which ceases upon her freedom. Since she freely chose her husband without coercion, she is considered to have ratified the marriage. The text addresses an objection concerning a similar scenario where an enslaved woman makes a purchase, which becomes void upon manumission; the distinction is that purchase is for the master's benefit, while marriage is for the woman's own right. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the marriage is null because manumission is not equivalent to the master's consent, but the Hanafi view prevails: the marriage is not a suspended contract in need of consent; it is simply obstructed by the master's right, which, once removed, allows the contract to operate. The passage further discusses the mahr: if the husband had intercourse before manumission, the mahr goes to the master; if after, to the woman. An objection that two mahrs should be due (one for the intercourse before valid marriage and the specified mahr) is answered by istihsan: only the specified mahr is due because the marriage's enforcement relates back to the contract, making the specification effective from that time.

Key Terms
نفذ (nafadha): To become effective or enforceable; here, the marriage is said to 'nafadha' (take effect) upon manumission.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; here, istihsan is used to override qiyas and deem only one mahr due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage is void.
B) The marriage becomes valid and she has the option to annul it.
C) The marriage becomes valid without any option for her.
D) The marriage remains suspended until the master consents.

Key Takeaway
An enslaved woman's marriage without permission becomes valid upon manumission with no option, distinguishing marriage from purchase due to the nature of the right transferred.

Father's Paternal Claim Over Son's Enslaved Woman
Ruling with proof: father's paternity claim and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a father's paternity claim when he has intercourse with his son's enslaved woman, and explain the Hanafi distinction between taking property without compensation (necessities) and with compensation (sexual needs) based on the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father'.

Core Idea
A father may claim paternity if he is a free Muslim and the enslaved woman was entirely owned by his son from conception; he must pay its value, unlike food and clothing.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling where a father's intercourse with his son's enslaved woman can establish lineage under strict conditions. We will explore the two conditions—father's free Muslim status and son's exclusive ownership from conception—and the prophetic hadith that authorizes the father's claim. We will also understand why the father must pay the value of the enslaved woman, contrasting with his right to take life necessities without compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the central act being ruled upon? The father has intercourse with his son's enslaved woman. If she gives birth and the father claims paternity, the child's lineage is established to the father.
2
State the Two Conditions for Validity
First, the father must be free and Muslim (a slave, mukatab, or non-Muslim has no guardianship over a Muslim). Second, the enslaved woman must be fully owned by the son from the time of conception until the claim.
Joint ownership or removal from son's ownership invalidates the claim.
3
Analyze the Hadith as the Legal Basis
The hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father' establishes the father's ownership over his son's property. The Hanafi school uses it to permit the father to take his son's property for his needs.
The hadith is ahad, so it is zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Distinguish Between Necessities and Sexual Needs
The father may take food and clothing without compensation (preserving life). For the enslaved woman (preserving lineage/sexual outlet), he must pay its value because the need is of lower degree.
Payment is wajib to protect the son's financial interest and avoid unlawful intercourse.
5
Understand the Role of Istanad (Retroactive Ownership)
For the paternity claim to be valid, the father's ownership of the slave woman is established retroactively to before intercourse, making the act lawful.
This ensures the intercourse is not considered zinā.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وطئ أمة ابنه ولو صغيراً فولدت أي بعد انقضاء مدة الحمل، فادعاه أي ادعى الأب الولد عند القاضي ثبت نسب الولد من الأب، ولصحة دعوة الأب شرطان: الأول: أن يكون الأب حراً مسلماً حتى لو كان عبداً أو مكاتباً أو كافراً لا تصح دعوته؛ لأنه لا ولاية له على المسلم، وكذا إذا كان مجنوناً، الثاني: أن تكون الأمة كلها في ملك الابن من وقت العلوق إلى حين الدعوة، فلو كانت مشتركة بينه وبين الأب أو غيره وجبت حصة الشريك من العقر وقيمة باقيها ولو حبلت في غير ملكه أو في ملكه وأخرجها الابن عن ملكه ثم استردّها لم تصح دعوته؛ لأن الملك إنما يثبت بطريق الاستناد إلى وقت العلوق فيستدعي قيام ولاية التملك من حين العلوق إلى التملك. والأصل فيه قوله عليه: "أنت ومالك لأبيك"، فبهذا الحديث يثبت للأب تملك مال ابنه للحاجة إلى بقاء النفس، فله تملك جاريته للحاجة إلى صيانة الماء غير أن الحاجة إلى بقاء النسل أدنى حالاً من الحاجة إلى بقاء النفس، فلذلك له تملك طعامه وثيابه ونحوهما بغير أداء القيمة، وله تملك جاريته بالقيمة إظهاراً للتفاوت بين الحاجتين، فيثبت له الملك قبل الوطء؛ لئلا يكون الوطء حراماً. (عيني، مستخلص)
And whoever has intercourse with his son's enslaved woman, even if the son is young, and she gives birth (meaning after the completion of the gestation period), then if he (the father) claims the child before the judge, the lineage of the child is established from the father. For the validity of the father's claim there are two conditions: First, the father must be free and Muslim; if he is a slave, or a mukatab, or a disbeliever, his claim is not valid because he has no guardianship over a Muslim; similarly if he is insane. Second, the entire enslaved woman must be in the ownership of the son from the time of conception until the time of the claim. If she was owned jointly between him and the father or someone else, the partner's share of the 'aqr (compensation for use) and the value of the remainder is obligatory. If she became pregnant while not in his ownership, or she was in his ownership but the son removed her from his ownership and then retrieved her, his claim is not valid because ownership is only established by relating back to the time of conception, which requires the existence of the capacity to own from the time of conception until ownership. The basis for this is his saying (peace be upon him): 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' By this hadith, the father's ownership of his son's property is established for the need of preserving life. Therefore, he may own the son's slave girl for the need of preserving his seed (i.e., lawful sexual outlet). However, the need to preserve progeny is of a lower degree than the need to preserve life. Hence, he may own the son's food and clothes and the like without paying compensation, but he may own his slave girl with compensation (payment of value) to show the difference between the two needs. Thus, his ownership is established before intercourse so that the intercourse is not unlawful. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where a father has intercourse with his son's enslaved woman, and a child is born. Under certain conditions, the father can claim paternity, and the child's lineage is established to him. Two conditions are essential: the father must be a free Muslim (a slave, mukatab, or non-Muslim cannot claim a Muslim child), and the enslaved woman must have been entirely owned by the son from the time of conception until the claim. If she was jointly owned, the father must pay the co-owner(s) their share of the compensation ('aqr) and the value. The legal basis is the prophetic hadith, 'You and your wealth belong to your father', which gives the father a right to his son's property to preserve his own life and lineage. However, there is a distinction: for necessities like food and clothing, the father may take without compensation; for sexual gratification via a slave girl, he must pay its value (to preserve the son's property rights) and to avoid unlawful intercourse. The father's ownership of the slave girl is retroactive to before the intercourse, legitimizing the act.

Key Terms
وطء (waṭʾ): Sexual intercourse.
عقر (ʿaqr): Compensation for sexual intercourse with a slave woman, typically the amount paid to the owner for the use of the slave's sexual capacity.
استناد (istinād): Attribution or relation back; here, ownership is established retroactively to the time of conception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Nothing, because the hadith gives him full ownership.
B) Only the value of the enslaved woman.
C) The value of the enslaved woman plus any co-owner's share of the 'aqr (if applicable).
D) Only the mahr-like compensation ('aqr) without paying the full value.

Key Takeaway
A father's paternity claim over a child born from his son's enslaved woman is valid only if the father is a free Muslim and the woman was solely owned by the son from conception; the father must pay the woman's value as compensation.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage of Slave-Girl Freed After Unauthorized Marriage
Ruling on validity and option of freed slave-girl married without master's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a slave-girl's marriage contracted without master's permission when she is freed before the master's rejection, and explain the reasoning (istihsan) that distinguishes it from the case of a purchase.

Core Idea
A slave-girl's marriage without master's permission becomes valid upon her freedom; she has no option to annul.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a slave-girl married without her master's permission who is subsequently freed before the master rejects the marriage. The Hanafi school holds that the marriage becomes valid upon her freedom and she has no right to annul it. This ruling is derived through istihsan (juristic preference) by distinguishing marriage from the analogous case of a purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
Note the two key parties: the slave-girl (the bride) and her master (the guardian). The marriage contract was made by someone else (e.g., her mother) without the master's permission.
2
Understand the initial impediment
The marriage contract is valid in substance (sahih) but its execution is suspended (mawquf) because the master's right to consent was not obtained. The master's right is the only impediment to the contract taking immediate effect.
In Hanafi law, a contract can be mawquf if it is contingent on a third party's authorization.
3
Analyze how the impediment is removed
When the slave-girl is freed, ownership is removed from the master. Consequently, the master loses his right over her, and the impediment to the marriage ceases. The contract then becomes fully effective (nafidh).
Freedom (itq) eliminates the master's right, not just his permission.
4
Differentiate the marriage case from the purchase case
The text anticipates an objection: why does the marriage become valid while a slave-girl's purchase without permission becomes void upon her freedom? The answer is that in a purchase, the bought item becomes the property of the master, so if it were to take effect after freedom, the slave-girl would acquire the item to her own benefit, harming the master. In marriage, the benefit (the right to marriage) is for the slave-girl from the outset, so no harm comes to the master. This distinction (tafriq) is the basis of istihsan.
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding a qiyas (analogy).
5
Evaluate the opposing view and conclude
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i argue that the marriage is void because emancipation does not constitute permission, and the master's guardianship ends after freedom. The Hanafi response is that the contract was not dependent on permission but on the master's right, which ceases by freedom. The Hanafi ruling is mufta bihi.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): Hanafis recognize the marriage without renewal; Shafi'is require a new contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرض في الأمة وإن كان العبد كذلك ليفرع مسألة الخيار ؛ لأنها تختص بالإماء دون العبيد، وصورة المسألة: أنه لو زوجت أمة بغير إذن مولاها ثم عتقت قبل رد المولى نكاحها صح النكاح؛ لأنها من أهل العبادة وكان امتناع النفوذ لحق المولى وقد زال حق المولى بإزالة الملك فينفذ ثم لا خيار لها؛ لأنها قد رضيت باختيارها إياه بلا جبر من المولى، فإن قيل: ينقض هذا بما إذا اشترت الأمة شيئاً ثم أعتقها المولى فإن الشراء يبطل؟ قلنا: إنما كان كذلك؛ لأن الشراء انعقد موجبا للملك للمولى حين اشترت فلو نفذ بعد عتقها كان موجبا للملك لها، فيتضرر من له الحق أي المولى، وأما هنا فانعقد العقد موجبا للحق لها فافترقا، ووجه عدم الخيار أيضاً أن نفوذ النكاح بعد العتق فلا يتصور ازدياد الملك عليها، وثبوت الخيار يكون باعتبار ازدياد الملك، وقال زفر والشافعي: بطل النكاح؛ لأنه كان موقوفا على إجازة المولى والإعتاق ليس بإجازة وبعد العتق ارتفعت ولايته فيبطل.
And it is assumed concerning the female slave, even if the male slave is likewise, to branch out the issue of option because it is specific to female slaves, not male slaves. The scenario: If a female slave is married without her master's permission, then she is freed before the master rejects her marriage, the marriage is valid because she is among those who perform worship, and the prevention of effect was for the right of the master, and the master's right has ceased by the removal of ownership, so it takes effect. Then she has no option because she has consented by her choice of him without compulsion from the master. If it is said: This is contradicted by the case when a female slave buys something, then the master frees her, for the purchase becomes void. We say: That is because the purchase was concluded obligating ownership for the master when she bought; if it were to take effect after her freedom, it would obligate ownership for her, causing harm to the one who has the right, i.e., the master. But here, the contract was concluded obligating the right for her, so they differ. The reason for no option also is that the marriage takes effect after freedom, so no increase of ownership over her is conceivable, and the establishment of option is based on increase of ownership. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The marriage is void because it was suspended on the master's permission, and emancipation is not permission, and after freedom his guardianship is removed, so it becomes void.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of a slave-girl's marriage contracted without her master's permission, followed by her emancipation. The Hanafi position is that the marriage becomes valid upon her freedom and she has no option to repudiate it. This is because the original impediment to the marriage's effect was the master's right, which ceases with emancipation. The author distinguishes this from a case where a slave-girl buys something; there, the purchase is for the master's benefit and would harm him if it became hers after freedom. In marriage, the benefit is for her from the start. Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree, arguing that emancipation does not constitute permission and the contract is void.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The right to annul a marriage contract after its conclusion; here, specifically for a freed slave-girl.
موقوف (mawquf): A contract that is suspended pending the permission of the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a slave-girl's marriage contracted without her master's permission if she is freed before the master rejects it?
A) The marriage is valid and she has the option to annul it.
B) The marriage is valid and she has no option.
C) The marriage is void automatically.
D) The marriage is suspended until she gains permission from the master.

Key Takeaway
A slave-girl's marriage without master's permission becomes valid upon her freedom because the master's right ceases; she has no option.

Mahr in Suspended Marriage: Recipient Based on Timing of Intercourse
Determination of mahr recipient based on timing of intercourse relative to freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who receives the mahr when a slave-girl is married without permission and later freed, and explain the istihsan basis for the single mahr payment.

Core Idea
Only one mahr is due – the named amount – because the contract’s enforcement relates back to its inception, overriding the analogy for two payments.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh literature addresses a nuanced case: a slave-girl is married without her master's permission, and later freed. The mahr obligation is clear, but who receives it depends on whether the husband had intercourse before or after her freedom. A hypothetical objection is raised that two mahr payments should be due, but the Hanafi school, using istihsan (juristic preference), rules that only one mahr, the named amount, is sufficient. Let’s explore the reasoning and the two scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Identify whether intercourse occurred before or after the slave-girl was freed. In each case, the mahr goes to a different recipient: to the master if before, to the freed woman if after.
The benefit of intercourse belongs to the owner at the time it occurs.
2
Understand the Objection for Two Mahr Payments
The text raises a hypothetical: since the marriage contract is initially suspended (on the master’s permission), one might argue that two mahr payments are due – mahr al-mithl for intercourse before the contract’s enforcement, plus the named mahr for the marriage itself.
This objection is based on strict analogy (qiyas).
3
Analyse the Qiyas Argument
The analogy compares this case to a man saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced' – there, two obligations arise. Similarly, here two mahr payments might seem required.
Qiyas would lead to a double obligation.
4
Explain the Istihsan Response
The Hanafi response departs from qiyas using istihsan: the contract’s enforcement upon the slave-girl’s freedom relates back (yanfa‘idu) to the time the contract was made. Thus, the named mahr at the time of the contract is valid and sufficient, avoiding double payment.
Istihsan is a recognised source in the Hanafi school, here overriding qiyas.
5
State the Final Hanafi Ruling
The mufta bihi position: only one mahr (the named amount) is owed. The recipient is determined solely by the timing of intercourse relative to freedom.
This is the relied-upon verdict for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمهر له وإلا لها أي لو وطئ الزوج هذه الأمة قبل عتقها فالمهر للمولى ؛ لاستيفائه المنافع المملوكة للمولى، وإن لم يطأها قبل العتق فالمهر للأمة المعتقة؛ لاستيفائه المنافع المملوكة لها، فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يجب مهران: مهر المثل بالدخول قبل إنفاذ النكاح بناء على أنه عقد موقوف ومهر بالنكاح وهو المسمى كما قال لأجنبية: إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق فتزوجها ودخل بها طلقت وعليه نصف المسمى في الطلاق قبل الدخول بحكم العقد ومهر بالدخول بعد الطلاق؟ قلنا: القياس هكذا، ولكن استحساناً أو جبنا مهراً واحداً وهو المسمى وقت العقد؛ لأن نفاذ العقد بالعتق يستند إلى وقت وجود العقد فصحت التسمية ووجب المسمى.
So the mahr is for him (the master), otherwise for her. That is, if the husband had intercourse with this slave-girl before her freedom, the mahr is for the master, because he utilized the benefits owned by the master. If he did not have intercourse before the freedom, then the mahr is for the freed slave-girl, because she utilized the benefits owned by her. If it is said: Two mahr payments should be obligated: the mahr al-mithl due to intercourse before the contract's enforcement, given it is a suspended contract, and the mahr of the named amount due to the marriage, as in the case of a man saying to a foreign woman: 'If I marry you then you are divorced,' then he marries her and has intercourse, she becomes divorced, and he owes half the named mahr for divorce before intercourse by virtue of the contract, and a mahr for intercourse after divorce. We say: The analogy is so, but by istihsan we make one mahr, the named one at the time of the contract, because the enforcement of the contract by freedom refers back to the time the contract existed, so the naming is valid and the named amount is obligatory.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage discusses the mahr in a case where a slave-girl was married without her master's permission, then freed. It distinguishes between two scenarios: if the husband had intercourse before her freedom, the mahr goes to the master because the benefit of the intercourse belonged to the master. If he did not have intercourse until after her freedom, the mahr goes to her because the benefit belongs to her. The author addresses a possible objection: one might think two mahr payments are due—one for the intercourse (mahr al-mithl) and one for the marriage (named mahr). The Hanafi response is that by istihsan, only one mahr is owed, the named one, because the contract's enforcement upon freedom relates back to the contract's inception, making the named mahr valid and sufficient.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard mahr for a woman of similar status, due when the named mahr is not specified or when intercourse occurs in a void marriage.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason, here to avoid double obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The freed slave-girl.
B) The master.
C) Both equally.
D) The husband keeps it.

Key Takeaway
Only one mahr is owed – the named amount – because the contract's enforcement upon freedom relates back to its inception, an application of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Father Claiming Paternity of Child from Son's Slave-Girl: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on father claiming paternity of child from son's slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for the father's paternity claim over his son's slave-girl's child, explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' and distinguish the Hanafi position from that of Zufar and al-Shafi'i regarding the payment of 'uqr.

Core Idea
If a father claims the child from his son's slave-girl, paternity is established, she becomes umm walad, and he pays her value — not 'uqr.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a delicate scenario: a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, a child is born, and the father claims paternity. The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling that balances the father's right to preserve his lineage with the son's property rights. The key proof is the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' which allows the father to take his son's property for necessity, but with gradation. In this lesson, we will analyze the conditions, the proof, the resulting obligations, and the scholarly dispute over whether 'uqr (compensation) is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine the scenario (father, son's slave-girl, child, claim) and the outcome: paternity established, slave-girl becomes umm walad, father pays value.
2
Extract the conditions for the claim
Two conditions: father must be a free Muslim; slave-girl must be wholly owned by son from conception to claim.
If conditions fail, claim is invalid.
3
Analyze the dalil (proof)
Examine the hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك' and how it justifies the father's right to the son's property for necessity, with gradation between life preservation and progeny preservation.
The hadith is ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Understand retroactive ownership
Ownership is established before intercourse to avoid unlawfulness. The need to preserve progeny justifies this retroactive establishment.
This is key to negating 'uqr.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar and al-Shafi'i require 'uqr; Abu Yusuf holds that she does not become umm walad. The Hanafi response uses the necessity argument.
Mufta bihi is the standard Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وطئ أمة ابنه ولو صغيراً فولدت أي بعد انقضاء مدة الحمل، فادعاه أي ادعى الأب الولد عند القاضي ثبت نسب الولد من الأب، ولصحة دعوة الأب شرطان: الأول: أن يكون الأب حراً مسلماً حتى لو كان عبداً أو مكاتباً أو كافراً لا تصح دعوته؛ لأنه لا ولاية له على المسلم، وكذا إذا كان مجنوناً، الثاني: أن تكون الأمة كلها في ملك الابن من وقت العلوق إلى حين الدعوة، فلو كانت مشتركة بينه وبين الأب أو غيره وجبت حصة الشريك من العقر وقيمة باقيها ولو حبلت في غير ملكه أو في ملكه وأخرجها الابن عن ملكه ثم استردّها لم تصح دعوته؛ لأن الملك إنما يثبت بطريق الاستناد إلى وقت العلوق فيستدعي قيام ولاية التملك من حين العلوق إلى التملك. والأصل فيه قوله عليه: "أنت ومالك لأبيك"، فبهذا الحديث يثبت للأب تملك مال ابنه للحاجة إلى بقاء النفس، فله تملك جاريته للحاجة إلى صيانة الماء غير أن الحاجة إلى بقاء النسل أدنى حالاً من الحاجة إلى بقاء النفس، فلذلك له تملك طعامه وثيابه ونحوهما بغير أداء القيمة، وله تملك جاريته بالقيمة إظهاراً للتفاوت بين الحاجتين، فيثبت له الملك قبل الوطء؛ لئلا يكون الوطء حراماً. (عيني، مستخلص) فادعاه ثبت نسبه منه، وصارت أمّ ولده وعليه قيمتها لا عُقرها وقيمة ولدها، أي الأب قيمة الولد أي الأمة أي يجب على الأب ودعوة الجدّ كدعوة الأب حال عدمه، ولو زوّجها أباه وولدت لم تصر أم ولده أي أب الأب الابن أمته أي الأمة أي أم ولد الأب فادعاه أي الأب ادعى الولد لنفسه. نسبه منه أي نسب الولد من الأب صيانة لمائه عن الضياع ولنفسه من الزنا. (ع) أم ولده أي أم ولد الأب لثبوت النسب منه سواء ادعى شبهة أم لا، صدق الابن فيه أم لا. (ط، ع) وعليه قيمتها: [ أي قيمة الجارية يوم علقت ولو معسراً صيانة لمال الولد مع حصول مقصود الوالد. (ط،ع) لا عقرها: [ أي لا يجب عليه عقر الأمة وهو مهرها. (ط، ع] أي يجب على الأب قيمة الأمة فقيراً كان أو موسراً؛ لأن الحاجة إلى إبقاء نسله قاصرة، فلهذا يملك أمته بقيمتها، ويحل له عند الحاجة الطعام لا الوطء، وفي "الأشباه" أن المراد من قيمتها ثمن مثلها لا قيمتها بالغة ما بلغت، وهذه القيمة تعتبر يوم علقت أي الوقت الذي اتصل به العلوق، وقوله: "لا عقرها" لأن الملك يثبت للأب قبيل الاستيلاد شرطاً له، فتبين أنه وطئ في ملك نفسه، فلا يجب عليه المهر، وقال زفر والشافعي: يجب العقر؛ لأن الوطء وجد في غير الملك. كة، إذ الملك إنما يثبت ضرورة تصحيح الاستيلاد صيانة لمائه عن الضياع فيثبت الملك قبيل العلوق، فلا ضرورة في نقله إلى حال الوطء، ولنا أن المصحح للاستيلاد حقيقة الملك لكونها للأب أو حق الملك لكونها مشتركـ وكلاهما غير ثابت للأب فلا بد من تقديم الملك ليصح الاستيلاد بوقوع الوطء في ملكه، فلا يجب عليه العقر؛ لثبوت الملك بطريق الاستناد وقيد بالدعوة؛ لأن الاستيلاد لا يتحقق بدون دعوة الولد حتى إذا لم يدعه لا يثبت نسبه ولا تصير أم ولد له بل يكون الولد عبداً للابن. (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) وقيمة ولدها: أي لا يجب أيضاً على الأب قيمة ولدها؛ لأنه انعلق حراً لتقدم الملك عليه ما لم تكن مشتركة فتجب حصة الشريك، وقال أبو يوسف: إن الجارية لا تصير أم ولد له ويكون الولد حراً بالقيمة، أما عدم كونها أم ولد؛ لأن المصحح للاستيلاد حقيقة الملك أو حقه، وكلاهما غير ثابت للأب وأما كون الولد حراً؛ لأنه ملكه أخوه فيعتق عليه، وقال أيضاً عليه العقر؛ لخلو الوطء عن الملك، وسقط الحد للشبهة. (فتح) ودعوة الجد كدعوة الأب الدعوة في النسب بالكسر الادعاء، وفي الطعام بالفتح هي الدعاء، أي دعوة الجد عند عدم ولاية الأب كدعوة الأب، ويشترط أن يثبت ولاية الجد من وقت العلوق إلى وقت الدعوة حتى لو أتت بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت انتقال الولاية إليه لم تصح دعوته. رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٢/١] لما ذكرنا في الأب. أما أب الأم وسائر ذوي الأرحام فلا تصح دعوتهم؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهم في تلك المال عند الحاجة. (فتح) حال عدمه أي عدم الأب بالموت أو الكفر أو الرق أو الجنون، أما عند ثبوت ولايته فلا تصح دعوته. (ط،ع) ولو زوجها أباه ولو نكاحا فاسداً أو تزوجها الأب بأن كان الولد صغيراً. (ط) لم تصر أم ولده: لأن انتقالها إلى ملك الأب لصيانة مائه وقد صار مصونا بدونه فلا حاجة إليه وقال :زفر تصير أم ولده؛ لأنه إذا صارت أم ولد له بالفجور فبالنكاح أولى، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح النكاح؛ لأن ما له من الحق يمنع صحة النكاح، =
And whoever has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, even if the son is a minor, and she gives birth after the completion of the pregnancy period, and he claims the child before the judge, the child's lineage is established from the father. For the validity of the father's claim, there are two conditions: First, the father must be a free Muslim; if he is a slave, a mukatib, a disbeliever, or insane, his claim is invalid because he has no guardianship over a Muslim. Second, the slave-girl must be entirely in the son's ownership from the time of conception until the claim; if she was shared between the father and another, the share of the partner in the 'uqr and value is due; if she conceived outside his ownership or he removed her from his ownership then took her back, his claim is invalid because ownership is established by reference back to the time of conception, requiring the existence of the ability to acquire ownership from that time. The basis is his statement (peace be upon him): 'You and your property belong to your father.' By this hadith, the father is established to have the right to possess his son's property due to the need for preserving life; thus he may possess his slave-girl to preserve his seed. However, the need for preserving progeny is of a lower degree than the need for preserving life; therefore he may possess his son's food and clothes without paying their value, but he may possess his slave-girl only by paying its value, to show the difference between the two needs. Thus ownership is established before intercourse to avoid it being forbidden. So if he claims it, the child's lineage is established from him, and she becomes his umm walad, and he must pay her value, not her 'uqr, nor the value of her child. The grandfather's claim is like the father's claim in his absence. If he (the son) marries her to his father and she gives birth, she does not become umm walad of the father. etc.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage covers a complex scenario: a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, and the child is born. If the father claims paternity, the child's lineage is established, and the slave-girl becomes his umm walad (mother of his child). The father must pay the value of the slave-girl at the time of conception, not the 'uqr (dowry-like compensation) and not the value of the child. Two conditions are required for the claim: the father must be a free Muslim, and the slave-girl must have been wholly owned by the son from conception through the claim. The basis is the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' which allows the father to take his son's property for necessity, but with gradation: for minor needs like food, no payment; for major need like preserving progeny via slave-girl, he pays value. This ensures intercourse is not unlawful. If the father does not claim, the child is a slave of the son. Abu Yusuf differs on some points. The grandfather's claim is valid in the father's absence under similar conditions. If the son marries the slave-girl to his father, she does not become umm walad because the need for preserving seed is already fulfilled by the marriage.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bears her master's child; here, the father of the son becomes her master after she gives birth and is freed upon the master's death.
عقر ('uqr): Compensation for intercourse with a slave-girl not owned by the man; a kind of mahr for slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The 'uqr (compensation).
C) The value of the slave-girl.
D) Both the value of the slave-girl and the 'uqr.

Key Takeaway
When a father claims paternity of the child from his son's slave-girl, lineage is established, the slave becomes umm walad, and the father must pay her value, not 'uqr or child's value, based on the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father.'

Father's Claim of Paternity from Son's Slave Woman
Ruling on father claiming child from son's slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a father claims paternity of a child from his son's slave woman and explain why he pays value rather than dower.

Core Idea
When a father claims his son's slave woman's child, he pays her value, not dower, due to retroactive ownership.

This passage examines a specific scenario where a father claims paternity of a child born to his son's slave woman. How does Islamic law handle this situation? The Hanafi school has a nuanced ruling that balances preserving lineage and protecting property rights. We will dissect the ruling, its proof, and the scholarly disagreements surrounding it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the father's claim of paternity of a child from his son's slave woman. The ruling is that lineage is established, the woman becomes his umm walad, and he must pay her value (not dower).
The father must actually claim the child; without claim, no effects.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' and the principle of istihsan to preserve lineage.
These are ahad hadith, so thubut is zanni, but accepted in practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith grants the father rights over his child's property, including slave women. Istihsan validates retroactive ownership just before conception to make the intercourse lawful and lineage valid.
This avoids the child being born out of wedlock and protects the father's lineage.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must pay the dower because intercourse occurred without ownership. Abu Yusuf holds that the woman does not become umm walad.
These disagreements affect both the financial obligation and the woman's status.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's: the woman becomes umm walad, and the father pays value, not dower. This is supported by the need to validate lineage and by the hadith.
The tarjih is based on the greater objective of preserving lineage (hifz al-nasl).

Source Text

فادعاه ثبت نسبه منه، وصارت أمّ ولده وعليه قيمتها لا عُقرها وقيمة ولدها، أي الأب الولد الأب أي الأمة أي يجب على الأب فادعاه أي الأب ادعى الولد لنفسه. نسبه منه أي نسب الولد من الأب صيانة لمائه عن الضياع ولنفسه من الزنا. (ع) أم ولده أي أم ولد الأب لثبوت النسب منه سواء ادعى شبهة أم لا، صدق الابن فيه أم لا. (ط، ع) وعليه قيمتها: [ أي قيمة الجارية يوم علقت ولو معسراً صيانة لمال الولد مع حصول مقصود الوالد. (ط،ع) لا عقرها: [ أي لا يجب عليه عقر الأمة وهو مهرها. (ط، ع] أي يجب على الأب قيمة الأمة فقيراً كان أو موسراً؛ لأن الحاجة إلى إبقاء نسله قاصرة، فلهذا يملك أمته بقيمتها، ويحل له عند الحاجة الطعام لا الوطء، وفي "الأشباه" أن المراد من قيمتها ثمن مثلها لا قيمتها بالغة ما بلغت، وهذه القيمة تعتبر يوم علقت أي الوقت الذي اتصل به العلوق، وقوله: "لا عقرها" لأن الملك يثبت للأب قبيل الاستيلاد شرطاً له، فتبين أنه وطئ في ملك نفسه، فلا يجب عليه المهر، وقال زفر والشافعي: يجب العقر؛ لأن الوطء وجد في غير الملك. كة، إذ الملك إنما يثبت ضرورة تصحيح الاستيلاد صيانة لمائه عن الضياع فيثبت الملك قبيل العلوق، فلا ضرورة في نقله إلى حال الوطء، ولنا أن المصحح للاستيلاد حقيقة الملك لكونها للأب أو حق الملك لكونها مشتركـ وكلاهما غير ثابت للأب فلا بد من تقديم الملك ليصح الاستيلاد بوقوع الوطء في ملكه، فلا يجب عليه العقر؛ لثبوت الملك بطريق الاستناد وقيد بالدعوة؛ لأن الاستيلاد لا يتحقق بدون دعوة الولد حتى إذا لم يدعه لا يثبت نسبه ولا تصير أم ولد له بل يكون الولد عبداً للابن. (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) وقيمة ولدها: أي لا يجب أيضاً على الأب قيمة ولدها؛ لأنه انعلق حراً لتقدم الملك عليه ما لم تكن مشتركة فتجب حصة الشريك، وقال أبو يوسف: إن الجارية لا تصير أم ولد له ويكون الولد حراً بالقيمة، أما عدم كونها أم ولد؛ لأن المصحح للاستيلاد حقيقة الملك أو حقه، وكلاهما غير ثابت للأب وأما كون الولد حراً؛ لأنه ملكه أخوه فيعتق عليه، وقال أيضاً عليه العقر؛ لخلو الوطء عن الملك، وسقط الحد للشبهة. (فتح)
If he claims him, his lineage is established from him, and she becomes his umm walad, and upon him is her value, not her dower, and not the value of her child. (Explanation: the father, the child, the father, meaning the slave woman, meaning it is obligatory on the father.) He claimed him, i.e., the father claimed the child for himself. His lineage from him, i.e., the lineage of the child from the father is established to protect his semen from waste and himself from fornication. (Ayni) His umm walad, i.e., she becomes the father's umm walad due to the establishment of lineage from him, whether he claimed on the basis of resemblance or not, and whether the son believed him or not. (Tahawi, Ayni) And upon him is her value: meaning the value of the slave woman on the day she conceived, even if he is insolvent, to protect the property of the child while achieving the father's purpose. (Tahawi, Ayni) Not her dower: meaning he is not obliged to pay the dower of the slave woman, which is her bridal gift. Rather, the father must pay the value of the slave woman whether he is poor or wealthy, because the need to preserve his offspring is limited; thus he owns his slave woman by her value, and it becomes permissible for him when in need to eat, but not intercourse. In Al-Ashbah, it is stated that what is meant by her value is its market price, not its absolute value whatever it may be, and this value is considered on the day she conceived, i.e., the time when conception occurred. And his saying: 'Not her dower' because ownership is established for the father just before istilad as a condition for it, so it becomes clear that he had intercourse in his own ownership, thus he does not owe the dower. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: the dower is obligatory because intercourse occurred without ownership. However, ownership is established only by necessity to validate istilad to protect his semen from waste, so ownership is established just before conception, so there is no necessity to transfer it to the time of intercourse. Our proof is that the validator of istilad is real ownership because she belongs to the father, or right of ownership because she is jointly owned, and both are not established for the father, so it is necessary to advance ownership so that istilad is valid by the occurrence of intercourse in his ownership. Thus he does not owe the dower because ownership is established retroactively, and it is conditioned by the claim because istilad is not realized without claiming the child. If he does not claim him, his lineage is not established, nor does she become his umm walad, but rather the child becomes a slave of the son. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath) And the value of her child: meaning the father is also not obliged to pay the value of her child, because he was conceived free due to the precedence of ownership over him, unless she was jointly owned, then the share of the partner is due. Abu Yusuf said: The slave woman does not become his umm walad, and the child is free with payment of value. As for her not becoming umm walad, it is because the validator of istilad is real ownership or its right, and both are not established for the father. And as for the child being free, because he is his brother's property, so he becomes free upon him. He also said: upon him is the dower because intercourse was devoid of ownership, and the hadd penalty is dropped due to the doubt. (Fath)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on a specific scenario: a father claims paternity of a child born to his son's slave woman. The passage type is a ruling with proof. The commentary explains each element. First, if the father makes such a claim, the child's lineage is established to him, and the slave woman becomes his umm walad (mother of his child). This frees her upon his death and grants her certain rights. The father must pay the value of the slave woman (not her dower) and is not liable for the child's value unless the woman was co-owned. The value is assessed on the day of conception, and the father may eat from her earnings but not have intercourse. The reason the father does not owe the dower is that through istilad (causing pregnancy and claiming paternity), he is deemed to have owned her just before conception, making his intercourse lawful. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that he must pay the dower since intercourse occurred without ownership, but the Hanafi response is that ownership is established retroactively to validate the istilad, protecting the father's lineage from being wasted. The text also notes Abu Yusuf's differing view: he holds that the woman does not become umm walad, but the child is free upon payment of value, and the father must pay the dower. The relied-upon Hanafi position is the one stated first: the woman becomes umm walad, and the father pays the value, not the dower. The proof is based on the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' (narrated by Ahmad) and the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to preserve lineage.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master; upon the master's death, she becomes free.
عقر (‘uqr): The dower (mahr) of a slave woman; in this context, the obligatory payment for intercourse.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of causing a slave woman to bear a child and claiming paternity, which gives her the status of umm walad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what must the father pay when he claims paternity of a child from his son's slave woman?
A) The dower (عقر) of the slave woman
B) The value (قيمة) of the slave woman
C) The value of the child
D) Both the dower and the value

Key Takeaway
When a father claims paternity from his son's slave woman, he pays her value to establish lineage, and she becomes his umm walad, with ownership retroactively validated.

Grandfather's Claim of Paternity in Father's Absence
Ruling on grandfather's claim of paternity with conditions and exclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid grandfather's paternity claim in the Hanafi school and identify who cannot make such a claim.

Core Idea
A grandfather's claim of paternity equals the father's only if his guardianship predates conception and the father is absent.

This passage addresses the scenario where the father is absent through death, disbelief, slavery, or insanity. The Hanafi school extends the father's right to claim paternity to the paternal grandfather by qiyas (analogy), but with a critical condition: the grandfather must have had guardianship over the mother from the time of conception until the claim. We will unpack the ruling, its conditions, and why other relatives like the maternal grandfather are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the grandfather's claim of paternity in the father's absence. The Hanafi ruling is that it is valid (sahih) like the father's claim.
2
Locate the condition for validity
The grandfather's guardianship (wilayah) must be continuous from the time of conception until the claim. If the child is born less than six months after guardianship transferred to him, the claim is invalid because the child would have been conceived before he had authority.
This six-month rule is derived from the minimum period of pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Identify the excluded parties
The maternal grandfather and all distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) cannot validly claim paternity because they lack guardianship over the property (the mother or the child's lineage).
Only the paternal grandfather inherits the father's role in this context.
4
Understand the underlying proof (qiyas)
The grandfather is analogized to the father based on the common effective cause (illah) of having guardianship over the mother and the need to preserve lineage. The father's claim is established by hadith and istihsan; the same applies to the grandfather when the father is absent.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy) because the illah is explicit.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
This is a Hanafi position. Other schools, such as the Shafi'i, may not grant the grandfather this right and restrict paternity claims to the father alone or under stricter conditions.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودعوة الجدّ كدعوة الأب حال عدمه، الدعوة في النسب بالكسر الادعاء، وفي الطعام بالفتح هي الدعاء، أي دعوة الجد عند عدم ولاية الأب كدعوة الأب، ويشترط أن يثبت ولاية الجد من وقت العلوق إلى وقت الدعوة حتى لو أتت بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت انتقال الولاية إليه لم تصح دعوته. رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٢/١] لما ذكرنا في الأب. أما أب الأم وسائر ذوي الأرحام فلا تصح دعوتهم؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهم في تلك المال عند الحاجة. (فتح) حال عدمه أي عدم الأب بالموت أو الكفر أو الرق أو الجنون، أما عند ثبوت ولايته فلا تصح دعوته. (ط،ع)
And the claim of the grandfather is like the claim of the father in his absence. 'Da‘wah' in lineage with kasrah means claim, and in food with fatha means invitation. That is, the claim of the grandfather when the guardianship of the father is absent is like the claim of the father. It is a condition that the guardianship of the grandfather is established from the time of conception until the time of claim, so that if she gives birth to the child in less than six months from the time guardianship transfers to him, his claim is not valid. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/222) For what we mentioned regarding the father. As for the mother's father and all other distant relatives, their claim is not valid because they have no guardianship in that property when needed. (Fath) 'In his absence' means the absence of the father through death, disbelief, slavery, or insanity. As for when his guardianship is established, his claim is not valid. (Tahawi, Ayni)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that extends the father's right to claim paternity to the paternal grandfather when the father is absent. The passage type is a ruling with proof, with the proof being qiyas (analogy) to the father's claim. The grandfather's claim is valid only if the father is absent due to death, disbelief, slavery, or insanity. Additionally, a condition is that the grandfather's guardianship (wilayah) must have been continuous from the time of conception until the claim; if the child is born less than six months after the grandfather assumed guardianship, the claim is invalid because it would imply the child was conceived before he had authority. The mother's father and other distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) cannot make such a claim because they lack guardianship over the property. This ruling is based on the same reasoning as the father's claim: the need to preserve the lineage. The 'illah (effective cause) is guardianship and the necessity to protect the lineage, which applies to the grandfather but not to other relatives.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilayah): Legal guardianship over a person or property; here, the grandfather must have had guardianship from conception.
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant relatives who are not heirs by fixed shares but may inherit through radd; here they cannot claim paternity.
الدعوة (da‘wah): Meaning 'claim' (in lineage) with kasrah on the dal; distinguished from da‘wah (invitation) with fatha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The grandfather must be wealthy
C) The mother must consent
D) The child must be born after the grandfather's claim

Key Takeaway
The paternal grandfather's claim of paternity is valid in the father's absence only if his guardianship predates conception and the child is born at least six months later; maternal relatives cannot claim.

Core Ruling: No Umm Walad When Son Marries Slave Woman to Father
Ruling statement with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a son's slave woman does not become the father's umm walad when married to him, and explain the rationale based on the protection of lineage.

Core Idea
A son's slave woman does not become the father's umm walad because the father's lineage is already protected through marriage.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a specific scenario: a son owns a slave woman and marries her to his father. The key question is whether she becomes the father's umm walad (mother of his child) if she bears him a child. The Hanafi ruling is no, and the reason is subtle: the purpose of making a slave woman umm walad is to protect the father's lineage by establishing paternity, but here paternity is already established through the marriage contract itself, so there is no need for the additional status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the son giving his slave woman in marriage to his father. The ruling is that she does not become the father's umm walad.
This ruling applies even if the marriage is irregular (fasid) or if the son is a minor.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
The reason given in the text is that transferring her to the father's ownership (which would happen if she became umm walad) is meant to protect his semen (i.e., ensure his child is free and lineage protected). But here, protection is already achieved because the child born from the marriage is legitimate and free, so there is no need.
The Hanafi school sees umm walad as a remedy for paternity claims, not a reward for marriage.
3
Note the scope of the ruling
The ruling covers two scenarios: (a) the son explicitly marries her to his father, and (b) the father marries her when the son is a minor (i.e., the son's guardian marries her on his behalf). In both, she does not become umm walad.
The fasid marriage case is included to emphasize that even an irregular nikah does not trigger umm walad status.
4
Contrast with the default rule
If the father himself owned the slave woman and she bore him a child, she would become umm walad. The distinction hinges on ownership: here the son owns her, not the father.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position stated in the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زوّجها أباه ولو نكاحا فاسداً أو تزوجها الأب بأن كان الولد صغيراً. (ط) لم تصر أم ولده: لأن انتقالها إلى ملك الأب لصيانة مائه وقد صار مصونا بدونه فلا حاجة إليه وقال :زفر تصير أم ولده؛ لأنه إذا صارت أم ولد له بالفجور فبالنكاح أولى، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح النكاح؛ لأن ما له من الحق يمنع صحة النكاح، =
And if he (the son) marries her to his father, even with a void marriage, or the father marries her when the son is a minor, she does not become his (the father's) umm walad. That is because her transfer to the father's ownership was to protect his semen, and now it is already protected without this, so there is no need. Zufar said: She becomes his umm walad, because if she becomes his umm walad by fornication, then by marriage it is even more appropriate. Al-Shafi'i said: The marriage is not valid because the right he has (in the woman) prevents the validity of the marriage.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if a son marries his own slave woman to his father, even if the marriage is irregular (fasid) or if the father marries her when the son is a minor, the woman does not become the father's umm walad upon bearing a child. The reason given is that the purpose of transferring her to the father's ownership was to protect his lineage, but since the father's lineage is already protected through the marriage (and not through paternity claim), there is no need to give her the status of umm walad. This is a Hanafi position. Zufar (a Hanafi disciple) disagrees, reasoning by analogy: if she could become umm walad by fornication (through istilad), then by a valid marriage it should be even more so. Al-Shafi'i holds that the marriage itself is invalid because the son has a right (milkiyyah) over the woman that prevents the father from marrying her. The Hanafi response (implied) is that the son's ownership does not prevent the father from marrying her as a client (as a third party) but the umm walad status is not granted because the father's lineage is already protected without needing to make her free on his death. The passage shows how Hanafi law limits the application of umm walad to cases of paternity (istilad) rather than marriage.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwij): To give in marriage; here, the son gives his slave woman in marriage to his father.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An irregular marriage that lacks a condition but can be rectified; in this context, even an irregular marriage does not produce umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master and thus becomes free upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a son marries his slave woman to his father, and she bears a child, what happens to her status?
A) She becomes the father's umm walad and is freed on his death.
B) She does not become umm walad and remains the son's property.
C) The marriage is invalid and she is returned to the son.
D) She becomes free immediately upon giving birth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits umm walad status to cases where paternity is claimed through istilad, not through marriage when the woman is owned by another.

Nikah — Marriage

8 min
Disputes on the Status of the Slave Woman in Marriage to the Father
Dispute with proof and counter-proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Zufar and al-Shafi'i on this issue, explain the Hanafi responses, and identify the practical difference (thamarah) between the views.

Core Idea
Zufar's analogy with fornication fails because marriage already protects lineage; al-Shafi'i's objection on ownership is overridden by permission.

In addition to the Hanafi ruling, two notable objections arise. Zufar, a leading Hanafi disciple, argues by qiyas that if fornication can establish umm walad, then a valid marriage should do so even more. Al-Shafi'i argues that the son's ownership prohibits the father from marrying her. This lesson examines these counter-arguments, the Hanafi responses, and the practical consequences of each view.

Guided Path
1
State Zufar's qiyas argument
Zufar argues: a slave woman who commits fornication and bears a child to a man becomes his umm walad if the man claims paternity. This is established in Hanafi law. By analogy, if she marries the same man and bears a child, she should even more certainly become umm walad, because marriage is a stronger and more honorable bond than fornication.
This is an argument a min al-awla (a fortiori) based on qiyas.
2
Identify the Hanafi response to Zufar
The Hanafi response, implicit in the text, is that the need for umm walad is to protect lineage when paternity is claimed outside of marriage. In marriage, lineage is already established by the contract, so the protective function is unnecessary. Therefore, the analogy fails because the underlying reason (illa) is different.
The Hanafi view prioritizes the purpose (maqsad) over a formal analogy.
3
State al-Shafi'i's objection
Al-Shafi'i holds that the marriage itself is invalid. He argues that the son has a right (haqq) in the slave woman by virtue of ownership, and this right creates a prohibition that prevents the father from marrying her. Thus, the marriage never takes effect, and umm walad cannot arise.
Al-Shafi'i does not discuss umm walad because he deems the marriage void ab initio.
4
Present the Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i
The Hanafi school maintains that the son's ownership does not create a barrier to marriage; the father can marry the woman with the son's permission. Since the son gives her in marriage, his permission is explicit. The right (haqq) is not a legal impediment (mani) to the contract. Thus, the marriage is valid.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that a master may marry his slave to anyone, including relatives, as long as no definitive prohibition exists.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
According to the Hanafi view: the marriage is valid, she does not become umm walad, so the son retains ownership. According to Zufar: the marriage is valid and she becomes umm walad, so the father must pay her value and she is freed on his death. According to al-Shafi'i: the marriage is invalid, so she remains the son's property, and the child's status may be illegitimate. The practical difference is whether the father pays compensation and whether the woman is freed.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of Abu Hanifa (as in the matn), rejecting Zufar's qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he (the son) marries her to his father, even with a void marriage, or the father marries her when the son is a minor, she does not become his (the father's) umm walad. That is because her transfer to the father's ownership was to protect his semen, and now it is already protected without this, so there is no need. Zufar said: She becomes his umm walad, because if she becomes his umm walad by fornication, then by marriage it is even more appropriate. Al-Shafi'i said: The marriage is not valid because the right he has (in the woman) prevents the validity of the marriage.
Source pages p. 381

This passage presents a ruling: if a son marries his own slave woman to his father, even if the marriage is irregular (fasid) or if the father marries her when the son is a minor, the woman does not become the father's umm walad upon bearing a child. The reason given is that the purpose of transferring her to the father's ownership was to protect his lineage, but since the father's lineage is already protected through the marriage (and not through paternity claim), there is no need to give her the status of umm walad. Al-Shafi'i holds that the marriage itself is invalid because the son has a right (milkiyyah) over the woman that prevents the father from marrying her. The Hanafi response (implied) is that the son's ownership does not prevent the father from marrying her as a client (as a third party) but the umm walad status is not granted because the father's lineage is already protected without needing to make her free on his death. The passage shows how Hanafi law limits the application of umm walad to cases of paternity (istilad) rather than marriage.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Zufar's analogy with fornications fails because the illa (lineage protection) is already fulfilled in marriage, highlighting that qiyas must be grounded in identical illas.
